<div id="contentSub2"></div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Social-cultural system</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about a cultural system of behaviors, practices and ethics. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Religion_(disambiguation)" title="Religion (disambiguation)">Religion (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Religious" redirects here. For the term describing a type of monk or nun, see <a href="/wiki/Religious_(Western_Christianity)" title="Religious (Western Christianity)">Religious (Western Christianity)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Religious_denomination" title="Religious denomination">Religious denomination</a>.</div>

portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Religions_by_country_sidebar" title="Template:Religions by country sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Religions_by_country_sidebar" title="Template talk:Religions by country sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Religions_by_country_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table></span><span class="mobile"><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:16_religious_symbol.jpg"><img alt="16 religious symbol" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1163" decoding="async" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/16_religious_symbol.jpg/220px-16_religious_symbol.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/16_religious_symbol.jpg/330px-16_religious_symbol.jpg 1.5x, href="/wiki/File:16_religious_symbol.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Religious symbols: <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_religion" title="Yoruba religion">Iese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baha'i</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eckankar" title="Eckankar">Eckankar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wicca" title="Wicca">Wicca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unitarian_Universalism" title="Unitarian Universalism">Unitarian Universalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thelema" title="Thelema">Thelema</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tenrikyo" title="Tenrikyo">Tenrikyo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a></div></div></div></span></span>
<p>
<b>Religion</b> is usually defined as a <a href="/wiki/Social_system" title="Social system">social</a>-<a href="/wiki/Cultural_system" title="Cultural system">cultural system</a> of designated <a href="/wiki/Religious_behaviour" title="Religious behaviour">behaviors</a> and practices, <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">beliefs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Worldview" title="Worldview">worldviews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religious_text" title="Religious text">texts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacred_site" title="Sacred site">sanctified places</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prophecy" title="Prophecy">prophecies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethics_in_religion" title="Ethics in religion">ethics</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Religious_organization" title="Religious organization">organizations</a>, that generally relates humanity to <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(religion)" title="Transcendence (religion)">transcendental</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a> elements;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> however, there is no scholarly consensus over what precisely constitutes a religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nongbri_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nongbri-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Different religions may or may not contain various elements ranging from the <a href="/wiki/Divinity" title="Divinity">divine</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames190231_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames190231-4">[4]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacred" title="Sacred">sacred things</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDurkheim1915_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDurkheim1915-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tillich,_P._1957_p.1_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tillich,_P._1957_p.1-6">[6]</a></sup> a supernatural being or supernatural beings<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vergote_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-vergote-7">[7]</a></sup> or "some sort of ultimacy and transcendence that will provide norms and power for the rest of life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paul_James_and_Peter_Mandaville_2010_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paul_James_and_Peter_Mandaville_2010-8">[8]</a></sup> Religious practices may include <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">rituals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sermon" title="Sermon">sermons</a>, commemoration or veneration (of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deities" title="Deities">deities</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a>), <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">sacrifices</a>, <a href="/wiki/Festival" title="Festival">festivals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Banquet" title="Banquet">feasts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trance" title="Trance">trances</a>, <a href="/wiki/Initiation" title="Initiation">initiations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Funeral" title="Funeral">funerary services</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matrimony" title="Matrimony">matrimonial services</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">meditation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">prayer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dance" title="Dance">dance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Community_service" title="Community service">public service</a>, or other aspects of human <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a>. Religions have <a href="/wiki/Sacred_history" title="Sacred history">sacred histories</a> and <a href="/wiki/Narrative" title="Narrative">narratives</a>, which may be preserved in sacred scriptures, and <a href="/wiki/Religious_symbol" title="Religious symbol">symbols</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_places" title="Holy places">holy places</a>, that aim mostly to give a <a href="/wiki/Meaning_of_life" title="Meaning of life">meaning to life</a>. Religions may contain symbolic stories, which are sometimes said by followers to be true, that may also attempt to explain the <a href="/wiki/Creation_myth" title="Creation myth">origin of life</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Religious_cosmology" title="Religious cosmology">universe</a>, and other phenomena. Traditionally, <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a>, in addition to reason, has been considered a source of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_belief" title="Religious belief">religious beliefs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iep.utm.edu_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-iep.utm.edu-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are an estimated 10,000 distinct religions worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> About 84% of the world's population is affiliated with <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, or some form of <a href="/wiki/Folk_religion" title="Folk religion">folk religion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB2012_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB2012-11">[11]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">religiously unaffiliated</a> demographic includes those who do not identify with any particular religion, <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheists</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Agnosticism" title="Agnosticism">agnostics.</a> But many<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (August 2022)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> of the religiously unaffiliated still have various<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (August 2022)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> religious beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pew_Global_Unaffiliated_12/2012_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pew_Global_Unaffiliated_12/2012-12">[12]</a></sup> A portion of the population mostly located in Africa and Asia are members of <a href="/wiki/New_religious_movement" title="New religious movement">New religious movements</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barker1999_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-barker1999-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Religious_studies" title="Religious studies">study of religion</a> comprises a wide variety of academic disciplines, including <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_religion" title="Philosophy of religion">philosophy of religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Comparative_religion" title="Comparative religion">comparative religion</a>, and social scientific studies. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theories_of_religions" title="Theories of religions">Theories of religion</a> offer various explanations for the origins and workings of religion, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ontological" title="Ontological">ontological</a> foundations of religious <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Being" title="Being">being</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">belief</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Concept_and_etymology">Concept and etymology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sakyamuni,_Lao_Tzu,_and_Confucius_-_Google_Art_ProjectFXD.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5758" data-file-width="5601" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Sakyamuni%2C_Lao_Tzu%2C_and_Confucius_-_Google_Art_ProjectFXD.jpg/220px-Sakyamuni%2C_Lao_Tzu%2C_and_Confucius_-_Google_Art_ProjectFXD.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Sakyamuni%2C_Lao_Tzu%2C_and_Confucius_-_Google_Art_ProjectFXD.jpg/330px-Sakyamuni%2C_Lao_Tzu%2C_and_Confucius_-_Google_Art_ProjectFXD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Sakyamuni%2C_Lao_Tzu%2C_and_Confucius_-_Google_Art_ProjectFXD.jpg/440px-Sakyamuni%2C_Lao_Tzu%2C_and_Confucius_-_Google_Art_ProjectFXD.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sakyamuni,_Lao_Tzu,_and_Confucius_-_Google_Art_ProjectFXD.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> painting</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Huxisanxiaotu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="724" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Huxisanxiaotu.jpg/220px-Huxisanxiaotu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Huxisanxiaotu.jpg/330px-Huxisanxiaotu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Huxisanxiaotu.jpg/440px-Huxisanxiaotu.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Huxisanxiaotu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a href="/wiki/Three_laughs_at_Tiger_Brook" title="Three laughs at Tiger Brook">Three laughs at Tiger Brook</a>", a <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> (12th century) painting portraying three men representing <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> (Daoism), and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> laughing together.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Religio" title="Religio">Religio</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Religion" title="History of Religion">History of Religion</a></div>
<p>The term <i>religion</i> comes from both <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_language" title="Anglo-Norman language">Anglo Norman</a> (1200s AD) and means respect for sense of right, moral obligation, sanctity, what is sacred, reverence for the gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> It is ultimately derived from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Religio" title="Religio">religiō</a></i></span>. According to <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">religiō</i></span> comes from <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">relegere</i></span>: <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">re</i></span> (meaning "again") + <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">lego</i></span> (meaning "read"), where <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">lego</i></span> is in the sense of "go over", "choose", or "consider carefully". However, some modern scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Tom_Harpur" title="Tom Harpur">Tom Harpur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Campbell" title="Joseph Campbell">Joseph Campbell</a> have argued that <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">religiō</i></span> is derived from <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">religare</i></span>: <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">re</i></span> (meaning "again") + <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">ligare</i></span> ("bind" or "connect"), which was made prominent by <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">St. Augustine</a>, following the interpretation given by <a href="/wiki/Lactantius" title="Lactantius">Lactantius</a> in <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Divinae institutiones</i></span>, IV, 28.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The medieval usage alternates with <i>order</i> in designating bonded communities like those of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monastic_orders" title="Monastic orders">monastic orders</a>: "we hear of the 'religion' of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Golden_Fleece" title="Order of the Golden Fleece">Golden Fleece</a>, of a knight 'of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Order_of_Aviz" title="Order of Aviz">religion of Avys</a>'".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huizinga_Middle_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Huizinga_Middle-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Religi.C5.8D"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Religiō"><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Religiō</i></span></span></h3>
<p>In classic antiquity, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">religiō</i></span> broadly meant conscientiousness, sense of right, moral obligation, or duty to anything.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In the ancient and medieval world, the etymological Latin root <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">religiō</i></span> was understood as an individual virtue of worship in mundane contexts; never as doctrine, practice, or actual source of knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrison_Territories_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison_Territories-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roberts_Jon_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts_Jon-22">[22]</a></sup> In general, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">religiō</i></span> referred to broad social obligations towards anything including family, neighbors, rulers, and even towards God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50_great_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-50_great-23">[23]</a></sup> <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Religiō</i></span> was most often used by the ancient Romans not in the context of a relation towards gods, but as a range of general emotions such as hesitation, caution, anxiety, fear; feelings of being bound, restricted, inhibited; which arose from heightened attention in any mundane context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religio_roman_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-religio_roman-24">[24]</a></sup> The term was also closely related to other terms like <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">scrupulus</i></span> (which meant "very precisely"), and some Roman authors related the term <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">superstitio</i></span> (which meant too much fear or anxiety or shame) to <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">religiō</i></span> at times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religio_roman_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-religio_roman-24">[24]</a></sup> When <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">religiō</i></span> came into English around the 1200s as religion, it took the meaning of "life bound by monastic vows" or monastic orders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huizinga_Middle_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Huizinga_Middle-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50_great_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-50_great-23">[23]</a></sup> The compartmentalized concept of religion, where religious things were separated from worldly things, was not used before the 1500s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50_great_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-50_great-23">[23]</a></sup> The concept of religion was first used in the 1500s to distinguish the domain of the church and the domain of civil authorities; the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Augsburg" title="Peace of Augsburg">Peace of Augsburg</a> marks such instance,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50_great_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-50_great-23">[23]</a></sup> which has been described as "the first step on the road toward a European system of <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign states</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Julius Caesar used <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">religiō</i></span> to mean "obligation of an oath" when discussing captured soldiers making an oath to their captors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The Roman naturalist <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> used the term <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">religiō</i></span> on elephants in that they venerate the sun and the moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> used <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">religiō</i></span> as being related to <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">cultum deorum</i></span> (worship of the gods).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Threskeia"><span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">Threskeia</i></span></span></h3>
<p>In the ancient Greece, the Greek term <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">threskeia</i></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">θρησκεία</span></span>) was loosely translated into Latin as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">religiō</i></span> in late antiquity. <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">Threskeia</i></span> was sparsely used in classical Greece but became more frequently used in the writings of Josephus in the 1st century AD. It was used in mundane contexts and could mean multiple things from respectful fear to excessive or harmfully distracting practices of others; to cultic practices. It was often contrasted with the Greek word <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">deisidaimonia</i></span>, which meant too much fear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-threskeia_greece_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-threskeia_greece-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion_and_religions">Religion and religions</span></h3>
<p>The modern concept of religion, as an abstraction that entails distinct sets of beliefs or doctrines, is a recent invention in the English language. Such usage began with texts from the 17th century due to events such as the splitting of <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> and globalization in the age of exploration, which involved contact with numerous foreign cultures with non-European languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrison_Territories_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison_Territories-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roberts_Jon_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts_Jon-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Religion_enlightenment_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Religion_enlightenment-30">[30]</a></sup> Some argue that regardless of its definition, it is not appropriate to apply the term religion to non-Western cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dubuisson_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-dubuisson-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fitzgerald_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzgerald-32">[32]</a></sup> Others argue that using religion on non-Western cultures distorts what people do and believe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Meaning_and_End_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Meaning_and_End-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept of religion was formed in the 16th and 17th centuries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nongbri1_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nongbri1-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Religion_enlightenment1_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Religion_enlightenment1-35">[35]</a></sup> despite the fact that ancient sacred texts like the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, and others did not have a word or even a concept of religion in the original languages and neither did the people or the cultures in which these sacred texts were written.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> For example, there is no precise equivalent of religion in Hebrew, and <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> does not distinguish clearly between religious, national, racial, or ethnic identities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> One of its central concepts is <span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">halakha</a></i></span>, meaning the walk or path sometimes translated as law, which guides religious practice and belief and many aspects of daily life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WhitefordII2008_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-WhitefordII2008-39">[39]</a></sup> Even though the beliefs and traditions of Judaism are found in the ancient world, ancient Jews saw Jewish identity as being about an ethnic or national identity and did not entail a compulsory belief system or regulated rituals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burns_Jewish_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burns_Jewish-40">[40]</a></sup> In the 1st century AD Josephus had used the Greek term <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">ioudaismos</i></span> (Judaism) as an ethnic term and was not linked to modern abstract concepts of religion or a set of beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nongbri_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nongbri-3">[3]</a></sup> The very concept of "Judaism" was invented by the Christian Church,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> and it was in the 19th century that Jews began to see their ancestral culture as a religion analogous to Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burns_Jewish_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burns_Jewish-40">[40]</a></sup> The Greek word <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">threskeia</i></span>, which was used by Greek writers such as Herodotus and Josephus, is found in the New Testament. <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">Threskeia</i></span> is sometimes translated as "religion" in today's translations, however, the term was understood as generic "worship" well into the medieval period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nongbri_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nongbri-3">[3]</a></sup> In the Quran, the Arabic word <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Din_(Arabic)" title="Din (Arabic)">din</a></i></span> is often translated as religion in modern translations, but up to the mid-1600s translators expressed <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">din</i></span> as "law".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nongbri_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nongbri-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> word <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">dharma</a>, sometimes translated as religion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14.1A:_The_Nature_of_Religion_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-14.1A:_The_Nature_of_Religion-42">[42]</a></sup> also means law. Throughout classical <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dharma%C5%9B%C4%81stra" title="Dharmaśāstra">study of law</a> consisted of concepts such as <a href="/wiki/Pr%C4%81ya%C5%9Bcitta" title="Prāyaścitta">penance through piety</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C4%80c%C4%81ra" title="Ācāra">ceremonial as well as practical traditions</a>. Medieval Japan at first had a similar union between imperial law and universal or Buddha law, but these later became independent sources of power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though traditions, sacred texts, and practices have existed throughout time, most cultures did not align with Western conceptions of religion since they did not separate everyday life from the sacred. In the 18th and 19th centuries, the terms Buddhism, Hinduism, Taoism, Confucianism, and <a href="/wiki/World_religions" title="World religions">world religions</a> first entered the English language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Josephson_2_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Josephson_2-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Native Americans were also thought of as not having religions and also had no word for religion in their languages either.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Josephson_2_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Josephson_2-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> No one self-identified as a Hindu or Buddhist or other similar terms before the 1800s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> "Hindu" has historically been used as a geographical, cultural, and later religious identifier for people indigenous to the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brian111_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-brian111-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Throughout its long history, Japan had no concept of religion since there was no corresponding Japanese word, nor anything close to its meaning, but when American warships appeared off the coast of Japan in 1853 and forced the Japanese government to sign treaties demanding, among other things, freedom of religion, the country had to contend with this idea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Invention_Japan_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Invention_Japan-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-japan_Galen_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-japan_Galen-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philologist" title="Philologist">philologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Max_M%C3%BCller" title="Max Müller">Max Müller</a> in the 19th century, the root of the English word religion, the Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">religiō</i></span>, was originally used to mean only reverence for God or the gods, careful pondering of divine things, <a href="/wiki/Pietas" title="Pietas">piety</a> (which Cicero further derived to mean diligence).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Müller characterized many other cultures around the world, including Egypt, Persia, and India, as having a similar power structure at this point in history. What is called ancient religion today, they would have only called law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:16_religionist_symbols.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="784" data-file-width="680" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/16_religionist_symbols.png/220px-16_religionist_symbols.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/16_religionist_symbols.png/330px-16_religionist_symbols.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/16_religionist_symbols.png/440px-16_religionist_symbols.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:16_religionist_symbols.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Religious symbols from left to right, top to bottom: <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí Faith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eckankar" title="Eckankar">Eckankar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wicca" title="Wicca">Wicca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unitarian_Universalism" title="Unitarian Universalism">Unitarian Universalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thelema" title="Thelema">Thelema</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tenrikyo" title="Tenrikyo">Tenrikyo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Definition_of_religion" title="Definition of religion">Definition of religion</a></div>
<p>Scholars have failed to agree on a definition of religion. There are, however, two general definition systems: the sociological/functional and the phenomenological/philosophical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_Western">Modern Western</span></h3>
<p>The concept of religion originated in the <a href="/wiki/Modern_era" title="Modern era">modern era</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">West</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fitzgerald_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzgerald-32">[32]</a></sup> Parallel concepts are not found in many current and past cultures; there is no equivalent term for religion in many languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nongbri_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-Nongbri-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50_great_23-4"><a href="#cite_note-50_great-23">[23]</a></sup> Scholars have found it difficult to develop a consistent definition, with some giving up on the possibility of a definition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Others argue that regardless of its definition, it is not appropriate to apply it to non-Western cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dubuisson_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-dubuisson-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fitzgerald_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzgerald-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>An increasing number of scholars have expressed reservations about ever defining the essence of religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> They observe that the way the concept today is used is a particularly modern construct that would not have been understood through much of history and in many cultures outside the West (or even in the West until after the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Peace of Westphalia</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The MacMillan Encyclopedia of Religions states:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The very attempt to define religion, to find some distinctive or possibly unique essence or set of qualities that distinguish the religious from the remainder of human life, is primarily a Western concern. The attempt is a natural consequence of the Western speculative, intellectualistic, and scientific disposition. It is also the product of the dominant Western religious mode, what is called the Judeo-Christian climate or, more accurately, the theistic inheritance from Judaism, Christianity, and Islam. The theistic form of belief in this tradition, even when downgraded culturally, is formative of the <a href="/wiki/Dichotomy" title="Dichotomy">dichotomous</a> Western view of religion. That is, the basic structure of theism is essentially a distinction between a transcendent deity and all else, between the creator and his creation, between God and man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Clifford_Geertz" title="Clifford Geertz">Clifford Geertz</a> defined religion as a
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>[…] system of symbols which acts to establish powerful, pervasive, and long-lasting moods and motivations in men by formulating conceptions of a general order of existence and clothing these conceptions with such an aura of factuality that the moods and motivations seem uniquely realistic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeertz199387–125_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeertz199387–125-67">[67]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Alluding perhaps to Tylor's "deeper motive", Geertz remarked that
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>[…] we have very little idea of how, in empirical terms, this particular miracle is accomplished. We just know that it is done, annually, weekly, daily, for some people almost hourly; and we have an enormous ethnographic literature to demonstrate it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeertz199390_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeertz199390-68">[68]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The theologian <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Vergote" title="Antoine Vergote">Antoine Vergote</a> took the term supernatural simply to mean whatever transcends the powers of nature or human agency. He also emphasized the cultural reality of religion, which he defined as
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>[…] the entirety of the linguistic expressions, emotions and, actions and signs that refer to a supernatural being or supernatural beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vergote_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-vergote-7">[7]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Peter_Mandaville" title="Peter Mandaville">Peter Mandaville</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_James_(academic)" title="Paul James (academic)">Paul James</a> intended to get away from the modernist dualisms or dichotomous understandings of immanence/transcendence, spirituality/materialism, and sacredness/secularity. They define religion as
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>[…] a relatively-bounded system of beliefs, symbols and practices that addresses the nature of existence, and in which communion with others and Otherness is <i>lived</i> as if it both takes in and spiritually transcends socially-grounded ontologies of time, space, embodiment and knowing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paul_James_and_Peter_Mandaville_2010_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Paul_James_and_Peter_Mandaville_2010-8">[8]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>According to the <i>MacMillan Encyclopedia of Religions</i>, there is an experiential aspect to religion which can be found in almost every culture:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>[…] almost every known culture [has] a depth dimension in cultural experiences […] toward some sort of ultimacy and transcendence that will provide norms and power for the rest of life. When more or less distinct patterns of behavior are built around this depth dimension in a culture, this structure constitutes religion in its historically recognizable form. Religion is the organization of life around the depth dimensions of experience—varied in form, completeness, and clarity in accordance with the environing culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical">Classical</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%91%D1%83%D0%B4%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B0%D0%BF_%D0%A6%D1%8B%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4344" data-file-width="3190" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/%D0%91%D1%83%D0%B4%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B0%D0%BF_%D0%A6%D1%8B%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2.JPG/220px-%D0%91%D1%83%D0%B4%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B0%D0%BF_%D0%A6%D1%8B%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/%D0%91%D1%83%D0%B4%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B0%D0%BF_%D0%A6%D1%8B%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2.JPG/330px-%D0%91%D1%83%D0%B4%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B0%D0%BF_%D0%A6%D1%8B%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/%D0%91%D1%83%D0%B4%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B0%D0%BF_%D0%A6%D1%8B%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2.JPG/440px-%D0%91%D1%83%D0%B4%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B0%D0%BF_%D0%A6%D1%8B%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%91%D1%83%D0%B4%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B0%D0%BF_%D0%A6%D1%8B%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Budazhap Shiretorov (Будажап Цыреторов), the head shaman of the religious community Altan Serge (Алтан Сэргэ) in <a href="/wiki/Buryatia" title="Buryatia">Buryatia</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Schleiermacher" title="Friedrich Schleiermacher">Friedrich Schleiermacher</a> in the late 18th century defined religion as <i>das schlechthinnige Abhängigkeitsgefühl</i>, commonly translated as "the feeling of absolute dependence".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>His contemporary <a href="/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" title="Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel">Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel</a> disagreed thoroughly, defining religion as "the Divine Spirit becoming conscious of Himself through the finite spirit."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Edward_Burnett_Tylor" title="Edward Burnett Tylor">Edward Burnett Tylor</a> defined religion in 1871 as "the belief in spiritual beings".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archive.org_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-archive.org-72">[72]</a></sup> He argued that narrowing the definition to mean the belief in a supreme deity or judgment after death or <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idolatry</a> and so on, would exclude many peoples from the category of religious, and thus "has the fault of identifying religion rather with particular developments than with the deeper motive which underlies them". He also argued that the belief in spiritual beings exists in all known societies.
</p><p>In his book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Varieties_of_Religious_Experience" title="The Varieties of Religious Experience">The Varieties of Religious Experience</a></i>, the psychologist <a href="/wiki/William_James" title="William James">William James</a> defined religion as "the feelings, acts, and experiences of individual men in their solitude, so far as they apprehend themselves to stand in relation to whatever they may consider the divine".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames190231_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames190231-4">[4]</a></sup> By the term divine James meant "any object that is god<i>like</i>, whether it be a concrete deity or not"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames190234_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames190234-73">[73]</a></sup> to which the individual feels impelled to respond with solemnity and gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames190238_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames190238-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>The sociologist <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Durkheim" title="Émile Durkheim">Émile Durkheim</a>, in his seminal book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Elementary_Forms_of_the_Religious_Life" title="The Elementary Forms of the Religious Life">The Elementary Forms of the Religious Life</a></i>, defined religion as a "unified system of beliefs and practices relative to sacred things".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDurkheim1915_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDurkheim1915-5">[5]</a></sup> By sacred things he meant things "set apart and forbidden—beliefs and practices which unite into one single moral community called a Church, all those who adhere to them". <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacred" title="Sacred">Sacred things</a> are not, however, limited to gods or spirits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[note 1]</a></sup> On the contrary, a sacred thing can be "a rock, a tree, a spring, a pebble, a piece of wood, a house, in a word, anything can be sacred".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDurkheim191537_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDurkheim191537-76">[75]</a></sup> Religious beliefs, myths, dogmas and legends are the representations that express the nature of these sacred things, and the virtues and powers which are attributed to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDurkheim191540–41_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDurkheim191540–41-77">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Echoes of James' and Durkheim's definitions are to be found in the writings of, for example, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Ferr%C3%A9" title="Frederick Ferré">Frederick Ferré</a> who defined religion as "one's way of valuing most comprehensively and intensively".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> Similarly, for the theologian <a href="/wiki/Paul_Tillich" title="Paul Tillich">Paul Tillich</a>, faith is "the state of being ultimately concerned",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tillich,_P._1957_p.1_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tillich,_P._1957_p.1-6">[6]</a></sup> which "is itself religion. Religion is the substance, the ground, and the depth of man's spiritual life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>When religion is seen in terms of sacred, divine, intensive valuing, or ultimate concern, then it is possible to understand why scientific findings and philosophical criticisms (e.g., those made by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a>) do not necessarily disturb its adherents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aspects">Aspects</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs">Beliefs</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_beliefs" title="Religious beliefs">Religious beliefs</a></div>
<p>Traditionally, <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a>, in addition to reason, has been considered a source of religious beliefs. The interplay between faith and reason, and their use as perceived support for religious beliefs, have been a subject of interest to philosophers and theologians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iep.utm.edu_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-iep.utm.edu-9">[9]</a></sup> The origin of religious belief as such is an open question, with possible explanations including awareness of individual death, a sense of community, and dreams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mythology">Mythology</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">Mythology</a></div>
<p>The word <i>myth</i> has several meanings.
</p>
<ol><li>A traditional story of ostensibly historical events that serves to unfold part of the world view of a people or explain a practice, belief, or natural phenomenon;</li>
<li>A person or thing having only an imaginary or unverifiable existence; or</li>
<li>A metaphor for the spiritual potentiality in the human being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>Ancient <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheistic</a> religions, such as those of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Rome</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, are usually categorized under the heading of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">mythology</a>. Religions of pre-industrial peoples, or <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultures</a> in development, are similarly called myths in the <a href="/wiki/Anthropology_of_religion" title="Anthropology of religion">anthropology of religion</a>. The term myth can be used pejoratively by both religious and non-religious people. By defining another person's religious stories and beliefs as mythology, one implies that they are less real or true than one's own religious stories and beliefs. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Campbell" title="Joseph Campbell">Joseph Campbell</a> remarked, "Mythology is often thought of as <i>other people's</i> religions, and religion can be defined as mis-interpreted mythology."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>In sociology, however, the term myth has a non-pejorative meaning. There, myth is defined as a story that is important for the group whether or not it is objectively or provably true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> Examples include the <a href="/wiki/Resurrection" title="Resurrection">resurrection</a> of their real-life founder <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>, which, to Christians, explains the means by which they are freed from sin, is symbolic of the power of life over death, and is also said to be a historical event. But from a mythological outlook, whether or not the event actually occurred is unimportant. Instead, the <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">symbolism</a> of the death of an old life and the start of a new life is what is most significant. Religious believers may or may not accept such symbolic interpretations.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Practices">Practices</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Religious_behaviour" title="Religious behaviour">Religious behaviour</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cult_(religious_practice)" title="Cult (religious practice)">Cult (religious practice)</a></div>
<p>The practices of a religion may include <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">rituals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sermon" title="Sermon">sermons</a>, commemoration or veneration of a <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a> (god or <a href="/wiki/Goddess" title="Goddess">goddess</a>), <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">sacrifices</a>, <a href="/wiki/Festival" title="Festival">festivals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Banquet" title="Banquet">feasts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trance" title="Trance">trances</a>, <a href="/wiki/Initiation" title="Initiation">initiations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Funeral" title="Funeral">funerary services</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matrimony" title="Matrimony">matrimonial services</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">meditation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">prayer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religious_music" title="Religious music">religious music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religious_art" title="Religious art">religious art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sacred_dance" title="Sacred dance">sacred dance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Community_service" title="Community service">public service</a>, or other aspects of human culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OD_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-OD-85">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_organisation">Social organisation</span></h3>
<p>Religions have a societal basis, either as a living tradition which is carried by lay participants, or with an organized <a href="/wiki/Clergy" title="Clergy">clergy</a>, and a definition of what constitutes adherence or membership.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academic_study">Academic study</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Religious_studies" title="Religious studies">Religious studies</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classifications_of_religious_movements" title="Classifications of religious movements">Classifications of religious movements</a></div>
<p>A number of disciplines study the phenomenon of religion: <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Comparative_religion" title="Comparative religion">comparative religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_religion" title="History of religion">history of religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_origin_of_religions" title="Evolutionary origin of religions">evolutionary origin of religions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthropology_of_religion" title="Anthropology of religion">anthropology of religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychology_of_religion" title="Psychology of religion">psychology of religion</a> (including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurotheology" title="Neurotheology">neuroscience of religion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_psychology_of_religion" title="Evolutionary psychology of religion">evolutionary psychology of religion</a>), <a href="/wiki/Law_and_religion" title="Law and religion">law and religion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sociology_of_religion" title="Sociology of religion">sociology of religion</a>.
</p><p>Daniel L. Pals mentions eight classical theories of religion, focusing on various aspects of religion: <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magic_(paranormal)" title="Magic (paranormal)">magic</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Burnett_Tylor" title="Edward Burnett Tylor">E.B. Tylor</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J.G._Frazer" title="J.G. Frazer">J.G. Frazer</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Psychoanalysis" title="Psychoanalysis">psycho-analytic</a> approach of <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a>; and further <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Durkheim" title="Émile Durkheim">Émile Durkheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">Max Weber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mircea_Eliade" title="Mircea Eliade">Mircea Eliade</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E.E._Evans-Pritchard" title="E.E. Evans-Pritchard">E.E. Evans-Pritchard</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Clifford_Geertz" title="Clifford Geertz">Clifford Geertz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPals2006_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPals2006-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Michael_Stausberg" title="Michael Stausberg">Michael Stausberg</a> gives an overview of contemporary theories of religion, including <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science_of_religion" title="Cognitive science of religion">cognitive</a> and biological approaches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStausberg2009_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStausberg2009-87">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theories">Theories</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theories_of_religion" title="Theories of religion">Theories of religion</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sociology_of_religion" title="Sociology of religion">Sociological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthropology_of_religion" title="Anthropology of religion">anthropological</a> theories of religion generally attempt to explain the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Origin_of_religion" title="Origin of religion">origin</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_function" title="Social function">function of religion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> These theories define what they present as universal characteristics of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_belief" title="Religious belief">religious belief</a> and <a href="/wiki/Religious_behaviour" title="Religious behaviour">practice</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_and_development">Origins and development</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_religion" title="History of religion">History of religion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yazilikaya_B_12erGruppe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1315" data-file-width="1999" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Yazilikaya_B_12erGruppe.jpg/250px-Yazilikaya_B_12erGruppe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Yazilikaya_B_12erGruppe.jpg/375px-Yazilikaya_B_12erGruppe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Yazilikaya_B_12erGruppe.jpg/500px-Yazilikaya_B_12erGruppe.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yazilikaya_B_12erGruppe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Yaz%C4%B1l%C4%B1kaya" title="Yazılıkaya">Yazılıkaya</a> sanctuary in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, with the twelve gods of the underworld</div></div></div>
<p>The origin of religion is uncertain. There are a number of theories regarding the subsequent origins of religious practices.
</p><p>According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthropologists" title="Anthropologists">anthropologists</a> John Monaghan and Peter Just, "Many of the great world religions appear to have begun as revitalization movements of some sort, as the vision of a charismatic prophet fires the imaginations of people seeking a more comprehensive answer to their problems than they feel is provided by everyday beliefs. Charismatic individuals have emerged at many times and places in the world. It seems that the key to long-term success—and many movements come and go with little long-term effect—has relatively little to do with the prophets, who appear with surprising regularity, but more to do with the development of a group of supporters who are able to institutionalize the movement."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Development_of_religion" title="Development of religion">development of religion</a> has taken different forms in different cultures. Some religions place an emphasis on belief, while others emphasize practice. Some religions focus on the subjective experience of the religious individual, while others consider the activities of the religious community to be most important. Some religions claim to be universal, believing their <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">laws</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a> to be binding for everyone, while others are intended to be practiced only by a closely defined or localized group. In many places, religion has been associated with public institutions such as <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hospital" title="Hospital">hospitals</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Family" title="Family">family</a>, <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">political</a> hierarchies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>Anthropologists John Monoghan and Peter Just state that, "it seems apparent that one thing religion or belief helps us do is deal with problems of human life that are significant, persistent, and intolerable. One important way in which religious beliefs accomplish this is by providing a set of ideas about how and why the world is put together that allows people to accommodate anxieties and deal with misfortune."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_system">Cultural system</span></h4>
<p>While religion is difficult to define, one standard model of religion, used in <a href="/wiki/Religious_studies" title="Religious studies">religious studies</a> courses, was proposed by <a href="/wiki/Clifford_Geertz" title="Clifford Geertz">Clifford Geertz</a>, who simply called it a "cultural system".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> A critique of Geertz's model by <a href="/wiki/Talal_Asad" title="Talal Asad">Talal Asad</a> categorized religion as "an <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropological</a> category".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> Richard Niebuhr's (1894–1962) five-fold classification of the relationship between Christ and culture, however, indicates that religion and culture can be seen as two separate systems, though with some interplay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_constructionism">Social constructionism</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Theories_about_religions#Social_constructionism" title="Theories about religions">Social constructionism</a></div>
<p>One modern academic theory of religion, <a href="/wiki/Social_constructionism" title="Social constructionism">social constructionism</a>, says that religion is a modern concept that suggests all <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a> practice and <a href="/wiki/Worship" title="Worship">worship</a> follows a model similar to the <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">Abrahamic religions</a> as an orientation system that helps to interpret reality and define human beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vergote_89_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-vergote_89-94">[93]</a></sup> Among the main proponents of this theory of religion are Daniel Dubuisson, Timothy Fitzgerald, Talal Asad, and Jason Ānanda Josephson. The social constructionists argue that religion is a modern concept that developed from Christianity and was then applied inappropriately to non-Western cultures.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cognitive_science">Cognitive science</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science_of_religion" title="Cognitive science of religion">Cognitive science of religion</a></div>
<p>Cognitive science of religion is the study of religious thought and behavior from the perspective of the cognitive and evolutionary sciences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> The field employs methods and theories from a very broad range of disciplines, including: <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_psychology" title="Cognitive psychology">cognitive psychology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_psychology" title="Evolutionary psychology">evolutionary psychology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_anthropology" title="Cognitive anthropology">cognitive anthropology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_neuroscience" title="Cognitive neuroscience">cognitive neuroscience</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurobiology" title="Neurobiology">neurobiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ethology" title="Ethology">ethology</a>. Scholars in this field seek to explain how human minds acquire, generate, and transmit religious thoughts, practices, and schemas by means of ordinary cognitive capacities.
</p><p>Hallucinations and delusions related to religious content occurs in about 60% of people with <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a>. While this number varies across cultures, this had led to theories about a number of influential religious phenomena and possible relation to psychotic disorders. A number of prophetic experiences are consistent with psychotic symptoms, although retrospective diagnoses are practically impossible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> Schizophrenic episodes are also experienced by people who do not have belief in gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Religious content is also common in <a href="/wiki/Temporal_lobe_epilepsy" title="Temporal lobe epilepsy">temporal lobe epilepsy</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obsessive-compulsive_disorder" title="Obsessive-compulsive disorder">obsessive-compulsive disorder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup> Atheistic content is also found to be common with temporal lobe epilepsy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heilman_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heilman-102">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparativism">Comparativism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Comparative_religion" title="Comparative religion">Comparative religion</a></div>
<p>Comparative religion is the branch of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Study_of_religions" title="Study of religions">study of religions</a> concerned with the systematic comparison of the doctrines and practices of the world's religions. In general, the comparative study of religion yields a deeper understanding of the fundamental philosophical concerns of religion such as <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, and the nature and form of <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a>. Studying such material is meant to give one a richer and more sophisticated understanding of human beliefs and practices regarding the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacred" title="Sacred">sacred</a>, <a href="/wiki/Numinous" title="Numinous">numinous</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a> and <a href="/wiki/Divinity" title="Divinity">divine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the field of comparative religion, a common geographical classification<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB-104">[103]</a></sup> of the <a href="/wiki/Major_religious_groups" title="Major religious groups">main world religions</a> includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern_religions" title="Middle Eastern religions">Middle Eastern religions</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iranian_religions" title="Iranian religions">Iranian religions</a>), <a href="/wiki/Indian_religions" title="Indian religions">Indian religions</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_religions" title="East Asian religions">East Asian religions</a>, African religions, American religions, Oceanic religions, and classical Hellenistic religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB-104">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_religion" title="History of religion">History of religion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prevailing_religious_population_by_country_percentage.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="263" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Prevailing_religious_population_by_country_percentage.svg/450px-Prevailing_religious_population_by_country_percentage.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Prevailing_religious_population_by_country_percentage.svg/675px-Prevailing_religious_population_by_country_percentage.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Prevailing_religious_population_by_country_percentage.svg/900px-Prevailing_religious_population_by_country_percentage.svg.png 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prevailing_religious_population_by_country_percentage.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of <a href="/wiki/List_of_religious_populations" title="List of religious populations">major denominations and religions of the world</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the 19th and 20th centuries, the academic practice of <a href="/wiki/Comparative_religion" title="Comparative religion">comparative religion</a> divided religious belief into philosophically defined categories called world religions. Some academics <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Study_of_religion" title="Study of religion">studying the subject</a> have divided religions into three broad categories:
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/World_religions" title="World religions">world religions</a>, a term which refers to <a href="/wiki/Transculturation" title="Transculturation">transcultural</a>, international religions;</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_religions" title="Indigenous religions">indigenous religions</a>, which refers to smaller, culture-specific or nation-specific religious groups; and</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_religious_movements" title="New religious movements">new religious movements</a>, which refers to recently developed religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>Some recent scholarship has argued that not all types of religion are necessarily separated by mutually exclusive philosophies, and furthermore that the utility of ascribing a practice to a certain philosophy, or even calling a given practice religious, rather than cultural, political, or social in nature, is limited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pennington_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-pennington-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> The current state of psychological study about the nature of religiousness suggests that it is better to refer to religion as a largely invariant phenomenon that should be distinguished from cultural norms (i.e. religions).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2022)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphological_classification">Morphological classification</span></h3>
<p>Some scholars classify religions as either <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universalizing_religion" title="Universalizing religion">universal religions</a></i> that seek worldwide acceptance and actively look for new converts, such as Christianity, Islam, Buddhism and Jainism, while <i><a href="/wiki/Ethnic_religion" title="Ethnic religion">ethnic religions</a></i> are identified with a particular ethnic group and do not seek converts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hinnells_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hinnells-110">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> Others reject the distinction, pointing out that all religious practices, whatever their philosophical origin, are ethnic because they come from a particular culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographic_classification">Demographic classification</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Major_religious_groups" title="Major religious groups">Major religious groups</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_religious_populations" title="List of religious populations">List of religious populations</a></div>
<p>The five largest religious groups by world population, estimated to account for 5.8 billion people and 84% of the population, are Christianity, Islam, Buddhism, Hinduism (with the relative numbers for Buddhism and Hinduism dependent on the extent of <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretism</a>) and traditional folk religion.
</p>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A global poll in 2012 surveyed 57 countries and reported that 59% of the world's population identified as religious, 23% as <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">not religious</a>, 13% as convinced <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheists</a>, and also a 9% decrease in identification as religious when compared to the 2005 average from 39 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gia_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-gia-116">[115]</a></sup> A follow-up poll in 2015 found that 63% of the globe identified as religious, 22% as not religious, and 11% as convinced atheists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GallupInt2015_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-GallupInt2015-117">[116]</a></sup> On average, women are more religious than men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup> Some people follow multiple religions or multiple religious principles at the same time, regardless of whether or not the religious principles they follow traditionally allow for <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> A 2017 <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew</a> projection suggests that Islam will overtake Christianity as the plurality religion by 2075. Unaffiliated populations are projected to drop, even when taking disaffiliation rates into account, due to differences in birth rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Specific_religions">Specific religions</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_religions_and_spiritual_traditions" title="List of religions and spiritual traditions">List of religions and spiritual traditions</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abrahamic">Abrahamic</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moln%C3%A1r_%C3%81brah%C3%A1m_kik%C3%B6lt%C3%B6z%C3%A9se_1850.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1043" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Moln%C3%A1r_%C3%81brah%C3%A1m_kik%C3%B6lt%C3%B6z%C3%A9se_1850.jpg/220px-Moln%C3%A1r_%C3%81brah%C3%A1m_kik%C3%B6lt%C3%B6z%C3%A9se_1850.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Moln%C3%A1r_%C3%81brah%C3%A1m_kik%C3%B6lt%C3%B6z%C3%A9se_1850.jpg/330px-Moln%C3%A1r_%C3%81brah%C3%A1m_kik%C3%B6lt%C3%B6z%C3%A9se_1850.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Moln%C3%A1r_%C3%81brah%C3%A1m_kik%C3%B6lt%C3%B6z%C3%A9se_1850.jpg/440px-Moln%C3%A1r_%C3%81brah%C3%A1m_kik%C3%B6lt%C3%B6z%C3%A9se_1850.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moln%C3%A1r_%C3%81brah%C3%A1m_kik%C3%B6lt%C3%B6z%C3%A9se_1850.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The patriarch <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a> (by <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zsef_Moln%C3%A1r_(painter)" title="József Molnár (painter)">József Molnár</a>)</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">Abrahamic religions</a> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monotheistic" title="Monotheistic">monotheistic</a> religions which believe they descend from <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Judaism">Judaism</span></h4>
