<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Open_Torah_and_pointer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Open_Torah_and_pointer.jpg/220px-Open_Torah_and_pointer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Open_Torah_and_pointer.jpg/330px-Open_Torah_and_pointer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Open_Torah_and_pointer.jpg/440px-Open_Torah_and_pointer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Open_Torah_and_pointer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> is the primary sacred text of Judaism.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> is the oldest Abrahamic religion, originating in the people of <a href="/wiki/History_of_ancient_Israel_and_Judah" title="History of ancient Israel and Judah">ancient Israel and Judea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica.com_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica.com-124">[123]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> is its foundational text, and is part of the larger text known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>. It is supplemented by oral tradition, set down in written form in later texts such as the <a href="/wiki/Midrash" title="Midrash">Midrash</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a>. Judaism includes a wide corpus of texts, practices, theological positions, and forms of organization. Within Judaism there are a variety of movements, most of which emerged from <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_Judaism" title="Rabbinic Judaism">Rabbinic Judaism</a>, which holds that God revealed his laws and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/613_Mitzvot" title="613 Mitzvot">commandments</a> to <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a> on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biblical_Mount_Sinai" title="Biblical Mount Sinai">Mount Sinai</a> in the form of both the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Written</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oral_Torah" title="Oral Torah">Oral Torah</a>; historically, this assertion was challenged by various groups. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_people" title="Jewish people">Jewish people</a> were scattered after the destruction of the <a href="/wiki/Temple_in_Jerusalem" title="Temple in Jerusalem">Temple in Jerusalem</a> in 70 CE. Today there are about 13 million Jews, about 40 per cent living in Israel and 40 per cent in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> The largest <a href="/wiki/Jewish_religious_movements" title="Jewish religious movements">Jewish religious movements</a> are <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">Orthodox Judaism</a> (<a href="/wiki/Haredi_Judaism" title="Haredi Judaism">Haredi Judaism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modern_Orthodox_Judaism" title="Modern Orthodox Judaism">Modern Orthodox Judaism</a>), <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Judaism" title="Conservative Judaism">Conservative Judaism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">Reform Judaism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica.com_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-britannica.com-124">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ISR-2013-Jerusalem-Holy_Sepulchre-dome.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5000" data-file-width="5000" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/ISR-2013-Jerusalem-Holy_Sepulchre-dome.jpg/220px-ISR-2013-Jerusalem-Holy_Sepulchre-dome.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/ISR-2013-Jerusalem-Holy_Sepulchre-dome.jpg/330px-ISR-2013-Jerusalem-Holy_Sepulchre-dome.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/ISR-2013-Jerusalem-Holy_Sepulchre-dome.jpg/440px-ISR-2013-Jerusalem-Holy_Sepulchre-dome.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ISR-2013-Jerusalem-Holy_Sepulchre-dome.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jesus is the central figure of Christianity.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> is based on the life and teachings of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_of_Nazareth" title="Jesus of Nazareth">Jesus of Nazareth</a> (1st century) as presented in the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christianity_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christianity-126">[125]</a></sup> The Christian faith is essentially faith in Jesus as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christ" title="Christ">Christ</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christianity_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-Christianity-126">[125]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Son_of_God" title="Son of God">Son of God</a>, and as <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Savior</a> and Lord. Almost all Christians believe in the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>, which teaches the unity of <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">Father</a>, <a href="/wiki/God_the_Son" title="God the Son">Son</a> (Jesus Christ), and <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> as three persons in <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">one Godhead</a>. Most Christians can describe their faith with the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a>. As the religion of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> in the first millennium and of <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> during the time of colonization, Christianity has been propagated throughout the world via <a href="/wiki/Christian_mission" title="Christian mission">missionary work</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spread_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spread-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Charity_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Charity-128">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Service_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Service-129">[128]</a></sup> It is the <a href="/wiki/Major_religious_groups" title="Major religious groups">world's largest religion</a>, with about 2.3 billion followers as of 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> The main divisions of Christianity are, according to the number of adherents:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history.com_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-history.com-131">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>, led by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Rome" title="Bishop of Rome">Bishop of Rome</a> and the bishops worldwide in communion with him, is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communion_(Christian)" title="Communion (Christian)">communion</a> of 24 Churches <i><a href="/wiki/Sui_iuris" title="Sui iuris">sui iuris</a></i>, including the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin Church</a> and 23 <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Catholic_Churches" title="Eastern Catholic Churches">Eastern Catholic churches</a>, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maronite" title="Maronite">Maronite</a> Catholic Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history.com_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-history.com-131">[130]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern Christianity</a>, which include <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy" title="Eastern Orthodoxy">Eastern Orthodoxy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodoxy" title="Oriental Orthodoxy">Oriental Orthodoxy</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a>, separated from the Catholic Church in the 16th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> and is split into thousands of <a href="/wiki/Religious_denomination" title="Religious denomination">denominations</a>. Major branches of Protestantism include <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodism</a>, though each of these contain many different denominations or groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history.com_131-2"><a href="#cite_note-history.com-131">[130]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>There are also smaller groups, including:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Restorationism" title="Restorationism">Restorationism</a>, the belief that Christianity should be restored (as opposed to reformed) along the lines of what is known about the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apostolic_Age" title="Apostolic Age">apostolic early church</a>.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latter-day_Saint_movement" title="Latter-day Saint movement">Latter-day Saint movement</a>, founded by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith" title="Joseph Smith">Joseph Smith</a> in the late 1820s.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah's Witnesses">Jehovah's Witnesses</a>, founded in the late 1870s by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Taze_Russell" title="Charles Taze Russell">Charles Taze Russell</a>.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kaaba,_Makkah6.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Kaaba%2C_Makkah6.jpg/220px-Kaaba%2C_Makkah6.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Kaaba%2C_Makkah6.jpg/330px-Kaaba%2C_Makkah6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Kaaba%2C_Makkah6.jpg/440px-Kaaba%2C_Makkah6.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kaaba,_Makkah6.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tawaf" title="Tawaf">circumambulating</a> the <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a>, the most sacred site in <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheistic</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Islam_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Islam-132">[131]</a></sup> religion based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an">Quran</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Islam_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Islam-132">[131]</a></sup> one of the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_holy_books" title="Islamic holy books">holy books</a> considered by Muslims to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wahy" title="Wahy">revealed</a> by <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a>, and on the <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">teachings (hadith)</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_of_Islam" title="Prophets of Islam">Islamic prophet</a> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>-a major political and religious figure of the 7th century CE. Islam is based on the unity of all religious philosophies and accepts all of the <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">Abrahamic</a> prophets of Judaism, Christianity and other Abrahamic religions before <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>. It is the most widely practiced religion of <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, while Muslim-majority countries also exist in parts of <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Europe" title="Southeast Europe">Southeast Europe</a>. There are also several <a href="/wiki/Islamic_republic" title="Islamic republic">Islamic republics</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni Islam</a> is the largest denomination within Islam and follows the Qur'an, the ahadith (ar: plural of Hadith) which record the <a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a>, whilst placing emphasis on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahabah" title="Sahabah">sahabah</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia Islam</a> is the second largest denomination of Islam and its adherents believe that <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a> succeeded Muhammad and further places emphasis on Muhammad's family.</li>
<li>There are also Muslim revivalist movements such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muwahhidism" title="Muwahhidism">Muwahhidism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salafism" title="Salafism">Salafism</a>.</li></ul>
<p>Other denominations of Islam include <a href="/wiki/Nation_of_Islam" title="Nation of Islam">Nation of Islam</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibadi" title="Ibadi">Ibadi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quranism" title="Quranism">Quranism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mahdavia" title="Mahdavia">Mahdavia</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-denominational_Muslims" title="Non-denominational Muslims">non-denominational Muslims</a>. <a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabism</a> is the dominant Muslim <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maddhab" title="Maddhab">schools of thought</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Saudi_Arabia" title="Kingdom of Saudi Arabia">Kingdom of Saudi Arabia</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h4>
<p>Whilst Judaism, Christianity and Islam are commonly seen as the only three Abrahamic faiths, there are smaller and newer traditions which lay claim to the designation as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lotus_Temple_in_New_Delhi_03-2016.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3380" data-file-width="5070" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Lotus_Temple_in_New_Delhi_03-2016.jpg/220px-Lotus_Temple_in_New_Delhi_03-2016.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Lotus_Temple_in_New_Delhi_03-2016.jpg/330px-Lotus_Temple_in_New_Delhi_03-2016.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Lotus_Temple_in_New_Delhi_03-2016.jpg/440px-Lotus_Temple_in_New_Delhi_03-2016.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lotus_Temple_in_New_Delhi_03-2016.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí</a> Lotus Temple in Delhi</div></div></div>
<p>For example, the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí Faith</a> is a <a href="/wiki/New_religious_movement" title="New religious movement">new religious movement</a> that has links to the major Abrahamic religions as well as other religions (e.g. of Eastern philosophy). Founded in 19th-century Iran, it teaches the unity of all religious philosophies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bahai.org_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-bahai.org-134">[133]</a></sup> and accepts all of the prophets of Judaism, Christianity, and Islam as well as additional prophets (Buddha, Mahavira), including its founder <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27u%27ll%C3%A1h" title="Bahá'u'lláh">Bahá'u'lláh</a>. It is an offshoot of <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A1bism" title="Bábism">Bábism</a>. One of its divisions is the <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Orthodox Baháʼí Faith">Orthodox Baháʼí Faith</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 48–49">: 48–49 </span></sup>
</p><p>Even smaller regional Abrahamic groups also exist, including <a href="/wiki/Samaritanism" title="Samaritanism">Samaritanism</a> (primarily in Israel and the West Bank), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rastafari_movement" title="Rastafari movement">Rastafari movement</a> (primarily in Jamaica), and <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a> (primarily in Syria, Lebanon, and Israel). The Druze faith originally developed out of <a href="/wiki/Isma%27ilism" title="Isma'ilism">Isma'ilism</a>, and it has sometimes been considered an <a href="/wiki/Islamic_schools_and_branches" title="Islamic schools and branches">Islamic school</a> by some Islamic authorities, but Druze themselves do not identify as <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Incorporated-1996_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Incorporated-1996-136">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mandaeism" title="Mandaeism">Mandaeism</a>, sometimes also known as Sabianism (after the mysterious <a href="/wiki/Sabians" title="Sabians">Sabians</a> mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, a name historically claimed by several religious groups),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnostic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monotheistic" title="Monotheistic">monotheistic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_religion" title="Ethnic religion">ethnic religion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandaens_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mandaens-140">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 4">: 4 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ginza_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ginza-141">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1">: 1 </span></sup> Its adherents, the <a href="/wiki/Mandaeans" title="Mandaeans">Mandaeans</a>, consider <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> to be their chief prophet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandaens_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mandaens-140">[139]</a></sup> Mandaeans are the last surviving Gnostics from antiquity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGrath_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGrath-142">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beijing_China_Hall-of-Prayer-for-Good-Harvests-01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2783" data-file-width="3601" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Beijing_China_Hall-of-Prayer-for-Good-Harvests-01.jpg/220px-Beijing_China_Hall-of-Prayer-for-Good-Harvests-01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Beijing_China_Hall-of-Prayer-for-Good-Harvests-01.jpg/330px-Beijing_China_Hall-of-Prayer-for-Good-Harvests-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Beijing_China_Hall-of-Prayer-for-Good-Harvests-01.jpg/440px-Beijing_China_Hall-of-Prayer-for-Good-Harvests-01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beijing_China_Hall-of-Prayer-for-Good-Harvests-01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Heaven" title="Temple of Heaven">Temple of Heaven</a>, a Taoist temple complex in Beijing</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Asian">East Asian</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_religions" title="East Asian religions">East Asian religions</a></div>
<p>East Asian religions (also known as Far Eastern religions or Taoic religions) consist of several religions of East Asia which make use of the concept of Tao (in Chinese), Dō (in Japanese or Korean) or Đạo (in Vietnamese). They include:
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Taoism_and_Confucianism">Taoism and Confucianism</span></h4>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>, as well as Korean, Vietnamese, and Japanese religion influenced by Chinese thought.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Folk_religions">Folk religions</span></h4>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Chinese_folk_religion" title="Chinese folk religion">Chinese folk religion</a>: the indigenous religions of the <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a>, or, by <a href="/wiki/Metonymy" title="Metonymy">metonymy</a>, of all the populations of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_cultural_sphere" title="Chinese cultural sphere">Chinese cultural sphere</a>. It includes the syncretism of <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wuism" title="Wuism">Wuism</a>, as well as many new religious movements such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chen_Tao_(True_Way_Cult)" title="Chen Tao (True Way Cult)">Chen Tao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Falun_Gong" title="Falun Gong">Falun Gong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yiguandao" title="Yiguandao">Yiguandao</a>.</li>
<li>Other folk and new religions of <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Korean_shamanism" title="Korean shamanism">Korean shamanism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chondogyo" title="Chondogyo">Chondogyo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jeung_San_Do" title="Jeung San Do">Jeung San Do</a> in Korea; <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Philippine_folk_religions" title="Indigenous Philippine folk religions">indigenous Philippine folk religions</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>; <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shugendo" title="Shugendo">Shugendo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ryukyuan_religion" title="Ryukyuan religion">Ryukyuan religion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_new_religions" title="Japanese new religions">Japanese new religions</a> in Japan; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satsana_Phi" title="Satsana Phi">Satsana Phi</a> in Laos; <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_folk_religion" title="Vietnamese folk religion">Vietnamese folk religion</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cao_%C4%90%C3%A0i" title="Cao Đài">Cao Đài</a>, <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B2a_H%E1%BA%A3o" title="Hòa Hảo">Hòa Hảo</a> in Vietnam.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dharmic_religions">Dharmic religions</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Indian_religions" title="Indian religions">Indian religions</a> are practiced or were founded in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. They are sometimes classified as the <i>dharmic religions</i>, as they all feature <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">dharma</a>, the specific law of reality and duties expected according to the religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hinduism">Hinduism</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ganesh,_folk_art,_Bharatiya_Lok_Kala_Museum,_Udaipur,_India.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1340" data-file-width="2934" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ganesh%2C_folk_art%2C_Bharatiya_Lok_Kala_Museum%2C_Udaipur%2C_India.jpg/220px-Ganesh%2C_folk_art%2C_Bharatiya_Lok_Kala_Museum%2C_Udaipur%2C_India.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ganesh%2C_folk_art%2C_Bharatiya_Lok_Kala_Museum%2C_Udaipur%2C_India.jpg/330px-Ganesh%2C_folk_art%2C_Bharatiya_Lok_Kala_Museum%2C_Udaipur%2C_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ganesh%2C_folk_art%2C_Bharatiya_Lok_Kala_Museum%2C_Udaipur%2C_India.jpg/440px-Ganesh%2C_folk_art%2C_Bharatiya_Lok_Kala_Museum%2C_Udaipur%2C_India.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ganesh,_folk_art,_Bharatiya_Lok_Kala_Museum,_Udaipur,_India.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Folk depiction of <a href="/wiki/Ganesha" title="Ganesha">Ganesha</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bharatiya_Lok_Kala_Mandal" title="Bharatiya Lok Kala Mandal">Bharatiya Lok Kala Mandal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Udaipur" title="Udaipur">Udaipur</a>, India</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> is also called <i>Vaidika Dharma</i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">dharma</a></i> of the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klostermaier2010_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klostermaier2010-144">[143]</a></sup> It is a <a href="/wiki/Synecdoche" title="Synecdoche">synecdoche</a> describing the similar philosophies of <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishnavism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shaivism" title="Shaivism">Shaivism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hindu_denominations" title="Hindu denominations">related groups</a> practiced or founded in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. Concepts most of them share in common include <a href="/wiki/Karma" title="Karma">karma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caste" title="Caste">caste</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mantra" title="Mantra">mantras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yantra" title="Yantra">yantras</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dar%C5%9Bana" title="Darśana">darśana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[note 2]</a></sup> Hinduism is one of the most ancient of still-active religions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[145]</a></sup> with origins perhaps as far back as prehistoric times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[146]</a></sup> Hinduism is not a monolithic religion but a religious category containing dozens of separate philosophies amalgamated as <a href="/wiki/San%C4%81tana_Dharma" title="Sanātana Dharma">Sanātana Dharma</a>, which is the name by which Hinduism has been known throughout history by its followers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TVM_aps_temple.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/TVM_aps_temple.jpg/220px-TVM_aps_temple.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/TVM_aps_temple.jpg/330px-TVM_aps_temple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/TVM_aps_temple.jpg/440px-TVM_aps_temple.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TVM_aps_temple.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Padmanabhaswamy_Temple" title="Padmanabhaswamy Temple">Padmanabhaswamy Temple</a> houses the <a href="/wiki/Padmanabhaswamy_Temple_treasure" title="Padmanabhaswamy Temple treasure">Padmanabhaswamy Temple treasure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[147]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jainism">Jainism</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shravanabelagola_Bahubali_wideframe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3376" data-file-width="4503" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Shravanabelagola_Bahubali_wideframe.jpg/220px-Shravanabelagola_Bahubali_wideframe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Shravanabelagola_Bahubali_wideframe.jpg/330px-Shravanabelagola_Bahubali_wideframe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Shravanabelagola_Bahubali_wideframe.jpg/440px-Shravanabelagola_Bahubali_wideframe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shravanabelagola_Bahubali_wideframe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 10th century <a href="/wiki/Gommateshwara_statue" title="Gommateshwara statue">Gommateshwara statue</a> in <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a></div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, taught primarily by <a href="/wiki/Rishabhanatha" title="Rishabhanatha">Rishabhanatha</a> (the founder of <a href="/wiki/Ahimsa" title="Ahimsa">ahimsa</a>) is an ancient Indian religion that prescribes a path of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-violence" title="Non-violence">non-violence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anekantavada" title="Anekantavada">anekantavada</a> for all forms of living beings in this universe; which helps them to eliminate all the <a href="/wiki/Karma_in_Jainism" title="Karma in Jainism">Karmas</a>, and hence to attain freedom from the cycle of birth and death (<a href="/wiki/Sa%E1%B9%83s%C4%81ra_(Jainism)" title="Saṃsāra (Jainism)">saṃsāra</a>), that is, achieving <a href="/wiki/Moksha_(Jainism)" title="Moksha (Jainism)">nirvana</a>. Jains are found mostly in India. According to Dundas, outside of the Jain tradition, historians date the <a href="/wiki/Mahavira" title="Mahavira">Mahavira</a> as about contemporaneous with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a> in the 5th-century BCE, and accordingly the historical <a href="/wiki/Parshvanatha" title="Parshvanatha">Parshvanatha</a>, based on the c. 250-year gap, is placed in 8th or 7th century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDundas200230–31_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDundas200230–31-150">[148]</a></sup>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Digambara" title="Digambara">Digambara</a> Jainism (or sky-clad) is mainly practiced in South India. Their holy books are <a href="/wiki/Pravachanasara" title="Pravachanasara">Pravachanasara</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samayasara" title="Samayasara">Samayasara</a> written by their Prophets <a href="/wiki/Kundakunda" title="Kundakunda">Kundakunda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amritchandra" title="Amritchandra">Amritchandra</a> as their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jain_Agamas_(Digambara)" title="Jain Agamas (Digambara)">original canon</a> is lost.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shwetambara" title="Shwetambara">Shwetambara</a> Jainism (or white-clad) is mainly practiced in Western India. Their holy books are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jain_Agamas_(%C5%9Av%C4%93t%C4%81mbara)" title="Jain Agamas (Śvētāmbara)">Jain Agamas</a>, written by their Prophet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sthulibhadra" title="Sthulibhadra">Sthulibhadra</a>.</li></ul></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Buddhism">Buddhism</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Open_front_door_over_Wat_Mixay_and_praying_bhikkhus,_Vientiane,_Laos.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4480" data-file-width="6720" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Open_front_door_over_Wat_Mixay_and_praying_bhikkhus%2C_Vientiane%2C_Laos.jpg/220px-Open_front_door_over_Wat_Mixay_and_praying_bhikkhus%2C_Vientiane%2C_Laos.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Open_front_door_over_Wat_Mixay_and_praying_bhikkhus%2C_Vientiane%2C_Laos.jpg/330px-Open_front_door_over_Wat_Mixay_and_praying_bhikkhus%2C_Vientiane%2C_Laos.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Open_front_door_over_Wat_Mixay_and_praying_bhikkhus%2C_Vientiane%2C_Laos.jpg/440px-Open_front_door_over_Wat_Mixay_and_praying_bhikkhus%2C_Vientiane%2C_Laos.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Open_front_door_over_Wat_Mixay_and_praying_bhikkhus,_Vientiane,_Laos.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wat Mixay Buddhist shrine in <a href="/wiki/Vientiane" title="Vientiane">Vientiane</a>, Laos</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> was founded by <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Siddhartha Gautama</a> in the 5th century BCE. Buddhists generally agree that Gotama aimed to help <a href="/wiki/Sentient_beings_(Buddhism)" title="Sentient beings (Buddhism)">sentient beings</a> end their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dukkha" title="Dukkha">suffering (dukkha)</a> by understanding the <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">true nature of phenomena</a>, thereby escaping the cycle of suffering and rebirth (<a href="/wiki/Sa%E1%B9%83s%C4%81ra_(Buddhism)" title="Saṃsāra (Buddhism)">saṃsāra</a>), that is, achieving <a href="/wiki/Nirvana_(Buddhism)" title="Nirvana (Buddhism)">nirvana</a>.
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Theravada" title="Theravada">Theravada</a> Buddhism, which is practiced mainly in <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> and Southeast Asia alongside folk religion, shares some characteristics of Indian religions. It is based in a large collection of texts called the <a href="/wiki/Pali_Canon" title="Pali Canon">Pali Canon</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahayana</a> Buddhism (or the Great Vehicle) under which are a multitude of doctrines that became prominent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_China" title="Buddhism in China">in China</a> and are still relevant <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Vietnam" title="Buddhism in Vietnam">in Vietnam</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Korea" title="Buddhism in Korea">Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Japan" title="Buddhism in Japan">Japan</a> and to a lesser extent <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_the_West" title="Buddhism in the West">in Europe and the United States</a>. Mahayana Buddhism includes such disparate teachings as <a href="/wiki/Zen" title="Zen">Zen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pure_Land_Buddhism" title="Pure Land Buddhism">Pure Land</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Soka_Gakkai" title="Soka Gakkai">Soka Gakkai</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vajrayana" title="Vajrayana">Vajrayana</a> Buddhism first appeared in India in the 3rd century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup> It is currently most prominent in the Himalaya regions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup> and extends across all of Asia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup> (cf. <a href="/wiki/Mikky%C5%8D" title="Mikkyō">Mikkyō</a>).</li>
<li>Two notable new Buddhist sects are <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B2a_H%E1%BA%A3o" title="Hòa Hảo">Hòa Hảo</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Navayana" title="Navayana">Navayana</a> (<a href="/wiki/Dalit_Buddhist_movement" title="Dalit Buddhist movement">Dalit Buddhist movement</a>), which were developed separately in the 20th century.</li></ul></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sikhism">Sikhism</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Miniature_of_Guru_Nanak_from_Astronomical_treatise.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="712" data-file-width="602" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Miniature_of_Guru_Nanak_from_Astronomical_treatise.jpg/220px-Miniature_of_Guru_Nanak_from_Astronomical_treatise.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Miniature_of_Guru_Nanak_from_Astronomical_treatise.jpg/330px-Miniature_of_Guru_Nanak_from_Astronomical_treatise.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Miniature_of_Guru_Nanak_from_Astronomical_treatise.jpg/440px-Miniature_of_Guru_Nanak_from_Astronomical_treatise.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Miniature_of_Guru_Nanak_from_Astronomical_treatise.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 1840 miniature of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a></div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panentheistic" title="Panentheistic">panentheistic</a> religion founded on the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a> and ten successive <a href="/wiki/Sikh_gurus" title="Sikh gurus">Sikh gurus</a> in 15th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a>. It is the <a href="/wiki/Major_religious_groups" title="Major religious groups">fifth-largest</a> <a href="/wiki/Organized_religion" title="Organized religion">organized religion</a> in the world, with approximately 30 million Sikhs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[153]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sikh" title="Sikh">Sikhs</a> are expected to embody the qualities of a <i>Sant-Sipāhī</i>—a saint-soldier, have control over one's internal <a href="/wiki/Five_Thieves" title="Five Thieves">vices</a> and be able to be constantly immersed in virtues clarified in the <a href="/wiki/Guru_Granth_Sahib" title="Guru Granth Sahib">Guru Granth Sahib</a>. The principal beliefs of Sikhi are faith in <i><a href="/wiki/Waheguru" title="Waheguru">Waheguru</a></i>—represented by the phrase <i><a href="/wiki/Ik_Onkar" title="Ik Onkar">ik ōaṅkār</a></i>, meaning one God, who prevails in everything, along with a <a href="/wiki/Praxis_(process)" title="Praxis (process)">praxis</a> in which the Sikh is enjoined to engage in social reform through the pursuit of justice for all human beings.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indigenous_and_folk">Indigenous and folk</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chickasaw_Stomp_Dance_Demonstration.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2667" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Chickasaw_Stomp_Dance_Demonstration.jpg/220px-Chickasaw_Stomp_Dance_Demonstration.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Chickasaw_Stomp_Dance_Demonstration.jpg/330px-Chickasaw_Stomp_Dance_Demonstration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Chickasaw_Stomp_Dance_Demonstration.jpg/440px-Chickasaw_Stomp_Dance_Demonstration.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chickasaw_Stomp_Dance_Demonstration.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chickasaw Native cultural/religious dancing</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:134px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Image_from_page_976_of_%22Bulletin%22_(1901).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="798" data-file-width="848" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Image_from_page_976_of_%22Bulletin%22_%281901%29.jpg/132px-Image_from_page_976_of_%22Bulletin%22_%281901%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Image_from_page_976_of_%22Bulletin%22_%281901%29.jpg/198px-Image_from_page_976_of_%22Bulletin%22_%281901%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Image_from_page_976_of_%22Bulletin%22_%281901%29.jpg/264px-Image_from_page_976_of_%22Bulletin%22_%281901%29.jpg 2x" width="132"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Image_from_page_976_of_%22Bulletin%22_(1901).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Peyotists with their ceremonial tools</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:83px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SB_-_Altay_shaman_with_drum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="607" data-file-width="395" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/SB_-_Altay_shaman_with_drum.jpg/81px-SB_-_Altay_shaman_with_drum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/SB_-_Altay_shaman_with_drum.jpg/121px-SB_-_Altay_shaman_with_drum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/SB_-_Altay_shaman_with_drum.jpg/161px-SB_-_Altay_shaman_with_drum.jpg 2x" width="81"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SB_-_Altay_shaman_with_drum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Altay shaman in Siberia</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E6%96%87%E6%BE%B3_%E5%9F%8E%E9%9A%8D%E5%BB%9F.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2513" data-file-width="3467" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/%E6%96%87%E6%BE%B3_%E5%9F%8E%E9%9A%8D%E5%BB%9F.jpg/220px-%E6%96%87%E6%BE%B3_%E5%9F%8E%E9%9A%8D%E5%BB%9F.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/%E6%96%87%E6%BE%B3_%E5%9F%8E%E9%9A%8D%E5%BB%9F.jpg/330px-%E6%96%87%E6%BE%B3_%E5%9F%8E%E9%9A%8D%E5%BB%9F.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/%E6%96%87%E6%BE%B3_%E5%9F%8E%E9%9A%8D%E5%BB%9F.jpg/440px-%E6%96%87%E6%BE%B3_%E5%9F%8E%E9%9A%8D%E5%BB%9F.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E6%96%87%E6%BE%B3_%E5%9F%8E%E9%9A%8D%E5%BB%9F.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Temple to the <a href="/wiki/City_God_(China)" title="City God (China)">city god</a> of Wenao in <a href="/wiki/Magong" title="Magong">Magong</a>, Taiwan</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_religions" title="Indigenous religions">Indigenous religions</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_religion" title="Ethnic religion">folk religions</a> refers to a broad category of traditional religions that can be characterised by <a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">shamanism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestor_worship" title="Ancestor worship">ancestor worship</a>, where traditional means "indigenous, that which is aboriginal or foundational, handed down from generation to generation…".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[154]</a></sup> These are religions that are closely associated with a particular group of people, ethnicity or tribe; they often have no formal creeds or sacred texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pew_global_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-pew_global-157">[155]</a></sup> Some faiths are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syncretic" title="Syncretic">syncretic</a>, fusing diverse religious beliefs and practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-158">[156]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australian_Aboriginal_mythology" title="Australian Aboriginal mythology">Australian Aboriginal religions</a>.</li>
<li>Folk religions of the Americas: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_American_religion" title="Native American religion">Native American religions</a></li></ul>
<p>Folk religions are often omitted as a category in surveys even in countries where they are widely practiced, e.g. in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pew_global_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-pew_global-157">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_African">Traditional African</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brooklyn_Museum_1992.133.4_Figure_of_Shango_on_Horseback.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="1152" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Brooklyn_Museum_1992.133.4_Figure_of_Shango_on_Horseback.jpg/220px-Brooklyn_Museum_1992.133.4_Figure_of_Shango_on_Horseback.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Brooklyn_Museum_1992.133.4_Figure_of_Shango_on_Horseback.jpg/330px-Brooklyn_Museum_1992.133.4_Figure_of_Shango_on_Horseback.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Brooklyn_Museum_1992.133.4_Figure_of_Shango_on_Horseback.jpg/440px-Brooklyn_Museum_1992.133.4_Figure_of_Shango_on_Horseback.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brooklyn_Museum_1992.133.4_Figure_of_Shango_on_Horseback.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Shango" title="Shango">Shango</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Orisha" title="Orisha">Orisha</a> of fire, lightning, and thunder, in the <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_religion" title="Yoruba religion">Yoruba religion</a>, depicted on horseback</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_African_religion" title="Traditional African religion">Traditional African religion</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_diasporic_religions" title="African diasporic religions">African diasporic religions</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_African_religion" title="Traditional African religion">African traditional religion</a> encompasses the traditional religious beliefs of people in Africa. In West Africa, these religions include the <a href="/wiki/Akan_religion" title="Akan religion">Akan religion</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dahomey_mythology" title="Dahomey mythology">Dahomey (Fon) mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Efik_mythology" title="Efik mythology">Efik mythology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odinani" title="Odinani">Odinani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serer_religion" title="Serer religion">Serer religion (A ƭat Roog)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_religion" title="Yoruba religion">Yoruba religion</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Bushongo_mythology" title="Bushongo mythology">Bushongo mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mbuti_mythology" title="Mbuti mythology">Mbuti (Pygmy) mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lugbara_mythology" title="Lugbara mythology">Lugbara mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dinka_religion" title="Dinka religion">Dinka religion</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lotuko_mythology" title="Lotuko mythology">Lotuko mythology</a> come from central Africa. Southern African traditions include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akamba_mythology" title="Akamba mythology">Akamba mythology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masai_mythology" title="Masai mythology">Masai mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malagasy_mythology" title="Malagasy mythology">Malagasy mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_religion" title="San religion">San religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lozi_mythology" title="Lozi mythology">Lozi mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tumbuka_mythology" title="Tumbuka mythology">Tumbuka mythology</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zulu_mythology" title="Zulu mythology">Zulu mythology</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bantu_mythology" title="Bantu mythology">Bantu mythology</a> is found throughout central, southeast, and southern Africa. In north Africa, these traditions include <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Berber_religion" title="Traditional Berber religion">Berber</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_religion" title="Ancient Egyptian religion">ancient Egyptian</a>.
</p><p>There are also notable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_diasporic_religions" title="African diasporic religions">African diasporic religions</a> practiced in the Americas, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santeria" title="Santeria">Santeria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Candomble" title="Candomble">Candomble</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haitian_Vodun" title="Haitian Vodun">Vodun</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucumi_religion" title="Lucumi religion">Lucumi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umbanda" title="Umbanda">Umbanda</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Macumba" title="Macumba">Macumba</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Templo_de_fuego,_Baku,_Azerbaiy%C3%A1n,_2016-09-27,_DD_34.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4861" data-file-width="8383" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Templo_de_fuego%2C_Baku%2C_Azerbaiy%C3%A1n%2C_2016-09-27%2C_DD_34.jpg/220px-Templo_de_fuego%2C_Baku%2C_Azerbaiy%C3%A1n%2C_2016-09-27%2C_DD_34.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Templo_de_fuego%2C_Baku%2C_Azerbaiy%C3%A1n%2C_2016-09-27%2C_DD_34.jpg/330px-Templo_de_fuego%2C_Baku%2C_Azerbaiy%C3%A1n%2C_2016-09-27%2C_DD_34.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Templo_de_fuego%2C_Baku%2C_Azerbaiy%C3%A1n%2C_2016-09-27%2C_DD_34.jpg/440px-Templo_de_fuego%2C_Baku%2C_Azerbaiy%C3%A1n%2C_2016-09-27%2C_DD_34.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Templo_de_fuego,_Baku,_Azerbaiy%C3%A1n,_2016-09-27,_DD_34.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sacred flame at the <a href="/wiki/Ateshgah_of_Baku" title="Ateshgah of Baku">Ateshgah of Baku</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iranian">Iranian</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Iranian_religions" title="Iranian religions">Iranian religions</a> are ancient religions whose roots predate the <a href="/wiki/Islamization" title="Islamization">Islamization</a> of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Iran" title="Greater Iran">Greater Iran</a>. Nowadays these religions are practiced only by minorities.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a> is based on the teachings of prophet <a href="/wiki/Zoroaster" title="Zoroaster">Zoroaster</a> in the 6th century BCE. Zoroastrians worship the <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">creator</a> <a href="/wiki/Ahura_Mazda" title="Ahura Mazda">Ahura Mazda</a>. In Zoroastrianism, good and evil have distinct sources, with evil trying to destroy the creation of Mazda, and good trying to sustain it.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Kurdistan" title="Religion in Kurdistan">Kurdish religions</a> include the traditional beliefs of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yazidi" title="Yazidi">Yazidi</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birgül_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birgül-160">[158]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alevi" title="Alevi">Alevi</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahl-e_Haqq" title="Ahl-e Haqq">Ahl-e Haqq</a>. Sometimes these are labeled <a href="/wiki/Yazd%C3%A2nism" title="Yazdânism">Yazdânism</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_religious_movements">New religious movements</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_religious_movement" title="New religious movement">New religious movement</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_new_religious_movements" title="List of new religious movements">List of new religious movements</a></div>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí Faith</a> teaches the unity of all religious philosophies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bahai.org_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-bahai.org-134">[133]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cao_%C4%90%C3%A0i" title="Cao Đài">Cao Đài</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretistic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheistic</a> religion, established in <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> in 1926.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[159]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eckankar" title="Eckankar">Eckankar</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">pantheistic</a> religion with the purpose of making God an everyday reality in one's life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[160]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epicureanism" title="Epicureanism">Epicureanism</a> is a Hellenistic philosophy that is considered by many of its practitioners as a type of (sometimes non-theistic) religious identity. It has its own scriptures, a monthly "feast of reason" on the Twentieth, and considers friendship to be holy.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hindu_reform_movements" title="Hindu reform movements">Hindu reform movements</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Ayyavazhi" title="Ayyavazhi">Ayyavazhi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swaminarayan_Faith" title="Swaminarayan Faith">Swaminarayan Faith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ananda_Marga" title="Ananda Marga">Ananda Marga</a>, are examples of new religious movements within Indian religions.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Japanese_new_religions" title="Japanese new religions">Japanese new religions</a> <i>(shinshukyo)</i> is a general category for a wide variety of religious movements founded in Japan since the 19th century. These movements share almost nothing in common except the place of their founding. The largest religious movements centered in Japan include <a href="/wiki/Soka_Gakkai" title="Soka Gakkai">Soka Gakkai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tenrikyo" title="Tenrikyo">Tenrikyo</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seicho-No-Ie" title="Seicho-No-Ie">Seicho-No-Ie</a> among hundreds of smaller groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[161]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah's Witnesses">Jehovah's Witnesses</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Nontrinitarianism" title="Nontrinitarianism">non-trinitarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christian</a> Reformist movement sometimes described as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millenarian" title="Millenarian">millenarian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[162]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-Druidism" title="Neo-Druidism">Neo-Druidism</a> is a religion promoting harmony with nature, and drawing on the practices of the druids.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>There are various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_Pagan" title="Modern Pagan">Modern Pagan</a> movements that attempt to reconstruct or revive ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pagan" title="Pagan">pagan</a> practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[163]</a></sup> These include <a href="/wiki/Heathenry_(new_religious_movement)" title="Heathenry (new religious movement)">Heathenry</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenism_(religion)" title="Hellenism (religion)">Hellenism</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kemeticism" title="Kemeticism">Kemeticism</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Noahidism" title="Noahidism">Noahidism</a> is a monotheistic ideology based on the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Laws_of_Noah" title="Seven Laws of Noah">Seven Laws of Noah</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[164]</a></sup> and on their traditional interpretations within Rabbinic Judaism.</li>
<li>Some forms of <a href="/wiki/Parody_religion" title="Parody religion">parody religion</a> or fiction-based religion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davidsen2013_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davidsen2013-167">[165]</a></sup> like <a href="/wiki/Jediism" title="Jediism">Jediism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pastafarianism" title="Pastafarianism">Pastafarianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dudeism" title="Dudeism">Dudeism</a>, "Tolkien religion",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davidsen2013_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davidsen2013-167">[165]</a></sup> and others often develop their own writings, traditions, and cultural expressions, and end up behaving like traditional religions.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Satanism" title="Satanism">Satanism</a> is a broad category of religions that, for example, worship Satan as a deity (<a href="/wiki/Theistic_Satanism" title="Theistic Satanism">Theistic Satanism</a>) or use Satan as a symbol of carnality and earthly values (<a href="/wiki/LaVeyan_Satanism" title="LaVeyan Satanism">LaVeyan Satanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Satanic_Temple" title="The Satanic Temple">The Satanic Temple</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[166]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Scientology" title="Scientology">Scientology</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[167]</a></sup> is a religious movement that teaches that people are immortal beings who have forgotten their true nature. Its method of spiritual rehabilitation is a type of counseling known as <a href="/wiki/Auditing_(Scientology)" title="Auditing (Scientology)">auditing</a>, in which practitioners aim to consciously re-experience and understand painful or traumatic events and decisions in their past in order to free themselves of their limiting effects.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UFO_Religion" title="UFO Religion">UFO Religions</a> in which extraterrestrial entities are an element of belief, such as <a href="/wiki/Ra%C3%ABlism" title="Raëlism">Raëlism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aetherius_Society" title="Aetherius Society">Aetherius Society</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Vian_Summers" title="Marshall Vian Summers">Marshall Vian Summers</a>'s <i>New Message from God</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Unitarian_Universalism" title="Unitarian Universalism">Unitarian Universalism</a> is a religion characterized by support for a free and responsible search for truth and meaning, and has no accepted <a href="/wiki/Creed" title="Creed">creed</a> or <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[168]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wicca" title="Wicca">Wicca</a> is a neo-pagan religion first popularised in 1954 by British civil servant <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Gardner_(Wiccan)" title="Gerald Gardner (Wiccan)">Gerald Gardner</a>, involving the worship of a God and Goddess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[169]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Related_aspects">Related aspects</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Law_and_religion" title="Law and religion">Law and religion</a></div>
<p>The study of law and religion is a relatively new field, with several thousand scholars involved in law schools, and academic departments including political science, religion, and history since 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[170]</a></sup> Scholars in the field are not only focused on strictly legal issues about religious freedom or non-establishment, but also study religions as they are qualified through judicial discourses or legal understanding of religious phenomena. Exponents look at canon law, natural law, and state law, often in a comparative perspective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[172]</a></sup> Specialists have explored themes in Western history regarding Christianity and justice and mercy, rule and equity, and discipline and love.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[173]</a></sup> Common topics of interest include marriage and the family<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[174]</a></sup> and human rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[175]</a></sup> Outside of Christianity, scholars have looked at law and religion links in the Muslim Middle East<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[176]</a></sup> and pagan Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>Studies have focused on <a href="/wiki/Secularization" title="Secularization">secularization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[179]</a></sup> In particular, the issue of wearing religious symbols in public, such as headscarves that are banned in French schools, have received scholarly attention in the context of human rights and feminism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[180]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science">Science</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Faith_and_rationality" title="Faith and rationality">Faith and rationality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Relationship_between_religion_and_science" title="Relationship between religion and science">Relationship between religion and science</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">Epistemology</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Science</a> acknowledges <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a> and <a href="/wiki/Empirical_evidence" title="Empirical evidence">empirical evidence</a>; and religions include <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sacredness" title="Sacredness">sacredness</a> whilst also acknowledging <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_religion" title="Philosophy of religion">philosophical</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metaphysical" title="Metaphysical">metaphysical</a> explanations with regard to the study of the universe. Both science and religion are not monolithic, timeless, or static because both are complex social and cultural endeavors that have changed through time across languages and cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concepts of science and religion are a recent invention: the term religion emerged in the 17th century in the midst of colonization and globalization and the Protestant Reformation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nongbri_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-Nongbri-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrison_Territories_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison_Territories-21">[21]</a></sup> The term science emerged in the 19th century out of <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a> in the midst of attempts to narrowly define those who studied nature (<a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural science</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrison_Territories_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison_Territories-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cahan_Natural_Philosophy_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cahan_Natural_Philosophy-184">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSACM_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSACM-185">[183]</a></sup> and the phrase religion and science emerged in the 19th century due to the reification of both concepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrison_Territories_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison_Territories-21">[21]</a></sup> It was in the 19th century that the terms Buddhism, Hinduism, Taoism, and Confucianism first emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrison_Territories_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison_Territories-21">[21]</a></sup> In the ancient and medieval world, the etymological Latin roots of both science (<i>scientia</i>) and religion (<i>religio</i>) were understood as inner qualities of the individual or virtues, never as doctrines, practices, or actual sources of knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrison_Territories_21-6"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison_Territories-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>In general the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> gains knowledge by testing hypotheses to develop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theories" title="Theories">theories</a> through elucidation of facts or evaluation by <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a> and thus only answers <a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology" title="Physical cosmology">cosmological</a> questions about the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> that can be observed and measured. It develops <a href="/wiki/Theory" title="Theory">theories</a> of the world which best fit physically observed evidence. All scientific knowledge is subject to later refinement, or even rejection, in the face of additional evidence. Scientific theories that have an overwhelming preponderance of favorable evidence are often treated as <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> verities in general parlance, such as the theories of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> to explain respectively the mechanisms of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>.
</p><p>Religion does not have a method per se partly because religions emerge through time from diverse cultures and it is an attempt to find meaning in the world, and to explain humanity's place in it and relationship to it and to any posited entities. In terms of Christian theology and ultimate truths, people rely on reason, experience, scripture, and tradition to test and gauge what they experience and what they should believe. Furthermore, religious models, understanding, and metaphors are also revisable, as are scientific models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>Regarding religion and science, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> states (1940): "For science can only ascertain what is, but not what should be, and outside of its domain value judgments of all kinds remain necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_New_Republic_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_New_Republic-187">[185]</a></sup> Religion, on the other hand, deals only with evaluations of human thought and action; it cannot justifiably speak of facts and relationships between facts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_New_Republic_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_New_Republic-187">[185]</a></sup>…Now, even though the realms of religion and science in themselves are clearly marked off from each other, nevertheless there exist between the two strong reciprocal relationships and dependencies. Though religion may be that which determine the goals, it has, nevertheless, learned from science, in the broadest sense, what means will contribute to the attainment of the goals it has set up."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[186]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morality">Morality</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Morality_and_religion" title="Morality and religion">Morality and religion</a></div>
<p>Many religions have value frameworks regarding personal behavior meant to guide adherents in determining between right and wrong. These include the <a href="/wiki/Jainism#Core_beliefs" title="Jainism">Triple Jems of Jainism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism's</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halacha" title="Halacha">Halacha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam's</a> <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholicism" title="Catholicism">Catholicism's</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canon_law_(Catholic_Church)" title="Canon law (Catholic Church)">Canon Law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism's</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eightfold_Path" title="Eightfold Path">Eightfold Path</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theological_Aspects_of_the_Avesta" title="Theological Aspects of the Avesta">Zoroastrianism's</a> good thoughts, good words, and good deeds concept, among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>Religion and morality are not synonymous. While it is "an almost automatic assumption."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Elements_of_Moral_Philosophy_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Elements_of_Moral_Philosophy-190">[188]</a></sup> in Christianity, morality can have a <a href="/wiki/Secular_morality" title="Secular morality">secular basis</a>.
</p><p>The study of religion and morality can be contentious due to ethnocentric views on morality, failure to distinguish between in group and out group altruism, and inconsistent definitions of religiosity.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact">Impact</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_politics" title="Religion in politics">Religion in politics</a></div>
<p>Religion has had a significant impact on the political system in many countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[189]</a></sup> Notably, most Muslim-majority countries adopt various aspects of <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a>, the Islamic law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[190]</a></sup> Some countries even define themselves in religious terms, such as <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">The Islamic Republic of Iran</a>. The sharia thus affects up to 23% of the global population, or 1.57 billion people who are <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslims</a>. However, religion also affects political decisions in many western countries. For instance, in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, 51% of voters would be less likely to vote for a presidential candidate who did not believe in God, and only 6% more likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[191]</a></sup> Christians make up 92% of members of the US Congress, compared with 71% of the general public (as of 2014). At the same time, while 23% of U.S. adults are religiously unaffiliated, only one member of Congress (<a href="/wiki/Kyrsten_Sinema" title="Kyrsten Sinema">Kyrsten Sinema</a>, D-Arizona), or 0.2% of that body, claims no religious affiliation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[192]</a></sup> In most European countries, however, religion has a much smaller influence on politics<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[193]</a></sup> although it used to be much more important. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a> were illegal in many European countries until recently, following Christian (usually <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholicism" title="Catholicism">Catholic</a>) doctrine. Several <a href="/wiki/List_of_atheists_in_politics_and_law" title="List of atheists in politics and law">European leaders are atheists</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>'s former president <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Hollande" title="François Hollande">Francois Hollande</a> or Greece's prime minister <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Tsipras" title="Alexis Tsipras">Alexis Tsipras</a>). In Asia, the role of religion differs widely between countries. For instance, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> is still one of the most religious countries and religion still has a strong impact on politics, given that Hindu nationalists have been targeting minorities like the Muslims and the Christians, who historically<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2021)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> belonged to the lower castes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[194]</a></sup> By contrast, countries such as <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_China" title="Religion in China">China</a> or <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Japan" title="Religion in Japan">Japan</a> are largely secular and thus religion has a much smaller impact on politics.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Secularism">Secularism</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ranjitsingh.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="211" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Ranjitsingh.gif/170px-Ranjitsingh.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d6/Ranjitsingh.gif 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ranjitsingh.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ranjit_Singh" title="Ranjit Singh">Ranjit Singh</a> established <a href="/wiki/Ranjit_Singh#Secular_Sikh_rule" title="Ranjit Singh">secular rule</a> over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a> in the early 19th century.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">Secularism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Secularization" title="Secularization">Secularization</a></div>
<p>Secularization is the transformation of the politics of a society from close identification with a particular religion's values and institutions toward nonreligious values and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secular" title="Secular">secular</a> institutions. The purpose of this is frequently modernization or protection of the populations religious diversity.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Religion_economy.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="670" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Religion_economy.png/400px-Religion_economy.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Religion_economy.png/600px-Religion_economy.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/17/Religion_economy.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Religion_economy.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Average income correlates negatively with (self-defined) religiosity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gia_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-gia-116">[115]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_religion" title="Economics of religion">Economics of religion</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Religion_and_business" title="Religion and business">Religion and business</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wealth_and_religion" title="Wealth and religion">Wealth and religion</a></div>
<p>One study has found there is a negative correlation between self-defined religiosity and the wealth of nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WIN-Gallup_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-WIN-Gallup-197">[195]</a></sup> In other words, the richer a nation is, the less likely its inhabitants to call themselves religious, whatever this word means to them (Many people identify themselves as part of a religion (not irreligion) but do not self-identify as religious).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WIN-Gallup_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-WIN-Gallup-197">[195]</a></sup>
