</p><p>Sociologist and political economist <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">Max Weber</a> has argued that Protestant Christian countries are wealthier because of their <a href="/wiki/Protestant_work_ethic" title="Protestant work ethic">Protestant work ethic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[196]</a></sup> According to a study from 2015, <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a> hold the largest amount of wealth (55% of the total world wealth), followed by <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a> (5.8%), <a href="/wiki/Hindus" title="Hindus">Hindus</a> (3.3%) and <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> (1.1%). According to the same study it was found that adherents under the classification <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">Irreligion</a> or other religions hold about 34.8% of the total global wealth (while making up only about 20% of the world population, see section on classification).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[197]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impacts_of_religion_on_health" title="Impacts of religion on health">Impacts of religion on health</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mayo_Clinic" title="Mayo Clinic">Mayo Clinic</a> researchers examined the association between religious involvement and spirituality, and physical health, mental health, health-related quality of life, and other health outcomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[198]</a></sup> The authors reported that: "Most studies have shown that religious involvement and spirituality are associated with better health outcomes, including greater longevity, coping skills, and health-related quality of life (even during terminal illness) and less anxiety, depression, and suicide."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Religion_and_Medicine_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-Religion_and_Medicine-201">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>The authors of a subsequent study concluded that the influence of religion on health is largely beneficial, based on a review of related literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[200]</a></sup> According to academic James W. Jones, several studies have discovered "positive correlations between religious belief and practice and mental and physical health and longevity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[201]</a></sup>
</p><p>An analysis of data from the 1998 US General Social Survey, whilst broadly confirming that religious activity was associated with better health and well-being, also suggested that the role of different dimensions of spirituality/religiosity in health is rather more complicated. The results suggested "that it may not be appropriate to generalize findings about the relationship between spirituality/religiosity and health from one form of spirituality/religiosity to another, across denominations, or to assume effects are uniform for men and women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[202]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Violence">Violence</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religious_violence" title="Religious violence">Religious violence</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Islam_and_violence" title="Islam and violence">Islam and violence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianity_and_violence" title="Christianity and violence">Christianity and violence</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Judaism_and_violence" title="Judaism and violence">Judaism and violence</a></div>
<p>Critics like <a href="/wiki/Hector_Avalos" title="Hector Avalos">Hector Avalos</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[203]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Regina_Schwartz" title="Regina Schwartz">Regina Schwartz</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReginaSchwartz_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReginaSchwartz-206">[204]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hitchens" title="Christopher Hitchens">Christopher Hitchens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a> have argued that religions are inherently violent and harmful to society by using violence to promote their goals, in ways that are endorsed and exploited by their leaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hitchens_2007_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hitchens_2007-207">[205]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (September 2010)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawkins_2006_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawkins_2006-208">[206]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (September 2010)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Anthropologist Jack David Eller asserts that religion is not inherently violent, arguing "religion and violence are clearly compatible, but they are not identical." He asserts that "violence is neither essential to nor exclusive to religion" and that "virtually every form of religious violence has its nonreligious corollary."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CruelCreeds1_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-CruelCreeds1-209">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CruelCreeds2_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-CruelCreeds2-210">[208]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_sacrifice">Animal sacrifice</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Animal_sacrifice" title="Animal sacrifice">Animal sacrifice</a></div>
<p>Done by some (but not all) religions, <a href="/wiki/Animal_sacrifice" title="Animal sacrifice">animal sacrifice</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">ritual</a> killing and offering of an animal to appease or maintain favour with a <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a>. It has been banned in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[209]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Superstition">Superstition</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Superstition" title="Superstition">Superstition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magical_thinking" title="Magical thinking">Magical thinking</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Magic_and_religion" title="Magic and religion">Magic and religion</a></div>
<p>Greek and Roman pagans, who saw their relations with the gods in political and social terms, scorned the man who constantly trembled with fear at the thought of the gods (<i>deisidaimonia</i>), as a slave might fear a cruel and capricious master. The Romans called such fear of the gods <i>superstitio</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[210]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> historian <a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">Polybius</a> described superstition in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Rome</a> as an <i><a href="/wiki/Instrumentum_regni" title="Instrumentum regni">instrumentum regni</a></i>, an instrument of maintaining the cohesion of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[211]</a></sup>
</p><p>Superstition has been described as the non-rational establishment of cause and effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[212]</a></sup> Religion is more complex and is often composed of social institutions and has a moral aspect. Some religions may include superstitions or make use of magical thinking. Adherents of one religion sometimes think of other religions as superstition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[214]</a></sup> Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheists" title="Atheists">atheists</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deists" title="Deists">deists</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skeptics" title="Skeptics">skeptics</a> regard religious belief as superstition.
</p><p>The Roman Catholic Church considers superstition to be sinful in the sense that it denotes a lack of trust in the divine providence of God and, as such, is a violation of the first of the Ten Commandments. The <a href="/wiki/Catechism_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Catechism of the Catholic Church">Catechism of the Catholic Church</a> states that superstition "in some sense represents a perverse excess of religion" (para. #2110). "Superstition," it says, "is a deviation of religious feeling and of the practices this feeling imposes. It can even affect the worship we offer the true God, e.g., when one attributes an importance in some way magical to certain practices otherwise lawful or necessary. To attribute the efficacy of prayers or of sacramental signs to their mere external performance, apart from the interior dispositions that they demand is to fall into superstition. Cf. Matthew 23:16–22" (para. #2111)
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agnosticism_and_atheism">Agnosticism and atheism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">Atheism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agnosticism" title="Agnosticism">Agnosticism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">Irreligion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antireligion" title="Antireligion">Antireligion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">Humanism</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_atheism" title="Criticism of atheism">Criticism of atheism</a></div>
<p>The terms <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheist" title="Atheist">atheist</a> (lack of belief in any gods) and agnostic (belief in the unknowability of the existence of gods), though specifically contrary to theistic (e.g. Christian, Jewish, and Muslim) religious teachings, do not by definition mean the opposite of religious. There are religions (including Buddhism, Taoism, and Hinduism), in fact, that classify some of their followers as agnostic, atheistic, or <a href="/wiki/Nontheism" title="Nontheism">nontheistic</a>. The true opposite of religious is the word irreligious. <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">Irreligion</a> describes an absence of any religion; <a href="/wiki/Antireligion" title="Antireligion">antireligion</a> describes an active opposition or aversion toward religions in general.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interfaith_cooperation">Interfaith cooperation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Interfaith_dialogue" title="Interfaith dialogue">Interfaith dialogue</a></div>
<p>Because religion continues to be recognized in Western thought as a universal impulse,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[215]</a></sup> many religious practitioners<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (July 2016)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[216]</a></sup> have aimed to band together in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interfaith" title="Interfaith">interfaith</a> dialogue, cooperation, and <a href="/wiki/Religion_and_peacebuilding" title="Religion and peacebuilding">religious peacebuilding</a>. The first major dialogue was the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_World%27s_Religions" title="Parliament of the World's Religions">Parliament of the World's Religions</a> at the 1893 <a href="/wiki/World%27s_Columbian_Exposition" title="World's Columbian Exposition">Chicago World's Fair</a>, which affirmed universal values and recognition of the diversity of practices among different cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[217]</a></sup> The 20th century has been especially fruitful in use of interfaith dialogue as a means of solving ethnic, political, or even religious conflict, with <a href="/wiki/Christian%E2%80%93Jewish_reconciliation" title="Christian–Jewish reconciliation">Christian–Jewish reconciliation</a> representing a complete reverse in the attitudes of many Christian communities towards Jews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[218]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent interfaith initiatives include A Common Word, launched in 2007 and focused on bringing Muslim and Christian leaders together,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[219]</a></sup> the "C1 World Dialogue",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[220]</a></sup> the Common Ground initiative between Islam and Buddhism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[221]</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> sponsored "World Interfaith Harmony Week".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[223]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h3>
<p>Culture and religion have usually been seen as closely related.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14.1A:_The_Nature_of_Religion_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-14.1A:_The_Nature_of_Religion-42">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Paul_Tillich" title="Paul Tillich">Paul Tillich</a> looked at religion as the soul of culture and culture as the form or framework of religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[224]</a></sup> In his own words:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Religion as ultimate concern is the meaning-giving substance of culture, and culture is the totality of forms in which the basic concern of religion expresses itself. In abbreviation: religion is the substance of culture, culture is the form of religion. Such a consideration definitely prevents the establishment of a dualism of religion and culture. Every religious act, not only in organized religion, but also in the most intimate movement of the soul, is culturally formed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[225]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ernst_Troeltsch" title="Ernst Troeltsch">Ernst Troeltsch</a>, similarly, looked at culture as the soil of religion and thought that, therefore, transplanting a religion from its original culture to a foreign culture would actually kill it in the same manner that transplanting a plant from its natural soil to an alien soil would kill it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[226]</a></sup> However, there have been many attempts in the modern pluralistic situation to distinguish culture from religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[227]</a></sup> Domenic Marbaniang has argued that elements grounded on beliefs of a metaphysical nature (religious) are distinct from elements grounded on nature and the natural (cultural). For instance, language (with its grammar) is a cultural element while sacralization of language in which a particular religious scripture is written is more often a religious practice. The same applies to music and the arts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[228]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_religion" title="Criticism of religion">Criticism of religion</a></div>
<p>Criticism of religion is <a href="/wiki/Criticism" title="Criticism">criticism</a> of the ideas, the truth, or the practice of religion, including its political and social implications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[229]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">

<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">That is how, according to Durkheim, Buddhism is a religion. "In default of gods, Buddhism admits the existence of sacred things, namely, the <a href="/wiki/Four_Noble_Truths" title="Four Noble Truths">four noble truths</a> and the practices derived from them" <a href="#CITEREFDurkheim1915">Durkheim 1915</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-145"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-145">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Hinduism is variously defined as a religion, set of religious beliefs and practices, religious tradition etc. For a discussion on the topic, see: "Establishing the boundaries" in Gavin Flood (2003), pp. 1–17. <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Gu%C3%A9non" title="René Guénon">René Guénon</a> in his<i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Introduction_to_the_Study_of_the_Hindu_doctrines" title="Introduction to the Study of the Hindu doctrines">Introduction to the Study of the Hindu doctrines</a></i> (1921 ed.), Sophia Perennis, <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1067248974">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit;word-wrap:break-word}.mw-parser-output .citation q{quotes:"\"""\"""'""'"}.mw-parser-output .citation:target{background-color:rgba(0,127,255,0.133)}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-free a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-free <li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFCaesar2007">Caesar, Julius (2007). "Civil Wars – Book 1". <i>The Works of Julius Caesar: Parallel English and Latin</i>. Translated by McDevitte, W.A.; Bohn, W.S. Forgotten Books. pp. 377–378. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-60506-355-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-60506-355-3"><bdi>978-1-60506-355-3</bdi></a>. <q>Sic terror oblatus a ducibus, crudelitas in supplicio, nova religio iurisiurandi spem praesentis deditionis sustulit mentesque militum convertit et rem ad pristinam belli rationem redegit." – (Latin); "Thus the terror raised by the generals, the cruelty and punishments, the new obligation of an oath, removed all hopes of surrender for the present, changed the soldiers' minds, and reduced matters to the former state of war."- (English)</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Civil+Wars+%E2%80%93+Book+1&amp;rft.btitle=The+Works+of+Julius+Caesar%3A+Parallel+English+and+Latin&amp;rft.pages=377-378&amp;rft.pub=Forgotten+Books&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-60506-355-3&amp;rft.aulast=Caesar&amp;rft.aufirst=Julius&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFPliny_the_Elder">Pliny the Elder. <a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.02.0137%3Abook%3D8%3Achapter%3D1" rel="nofollow">"Elephants; Their Capacity"</a>. <i>The Natural History, Book VIII</i>. Tufts University. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210507142052/https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.02.0137%3Abook%3D8%3Achapter%3D1" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 7 May 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">21 February</span> 2021</span>. <q>maximum est elephans proximumque humanis sensibus, quippe intellectus illis sermonis patrii et imperiorum obedientia, officiorum quae didicere memoria, amoris et gloriae voluptas, immo vero, quae etiam in homine rara, probitas, prudentia, aequitas, religio quoque siderum solisque ac lunae veneratio." "The elephant is the largest of them all, and in intelligence approaches the nearest to man. It understands the language of its country, it obeys commands, and it remembers all the duties which it has been taught. It is sensible alike of the pleasures of love and glory, and, to a degree that is rare among men even, possesses notions of honesty, prudence, and equity; it has a religious respect also for the stars, and a veneration for the sun and the moon."</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Elephants%3B+Their+Capacity&amp;rft.btitle=The+Natural+History%2C+Book+VIII&amp;rft.pub=Tufts+University&amp;rft.au=Pliny+the+Elder&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.perseus.tufts.edu%2Fhopper%2Ftext%3Fdoc%3DPerseus%253Atext%253A1999.02.0137%253Abook%253D8%253Achapter%253D1&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-Nongbri1-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Nongbri1_34-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFNongbri2013">Nongbri, Brent (2013). <i>Before Religion: A History of a Modern Concept</i>. Yale University Press. p. 152. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-300-15416-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-300-15416-0"><bdi>978-0-300-15416-0</bdi></a>. <q>Although the Greeks, Romans, Mesopotamians, and many other peoples have long histories, the stories of their respective religions are of recent pedigree. The formation of ancient religions as objects of study coincided with the formation of religion itself as a concept of the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Before+Religion%3A+A+History+of+a+Modern+Concept&amp;rft.pages=152&amp;rft.pub=Yale+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-300-15416-0&amp;rft.aulast=Nongbri&amp;rft.aufirst=Brent&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Religion_enlightenment1-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Religion_enlightenment1_35-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFHarrison1990">Harrison, Peter (1990). <span class="cs1-lock-limited" title="Free access subject to limited trial, subscription normally required"><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/religionreligion00harr" rel="nofollow"><i><span></span>'Religion' and the Religions in the English Enlightenment</i></a></span>. Cambridge: Cambridge University Press. p. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/religionreligion00harr/page/n11" rel="nofollow">1</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-521-89293-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-521-89293-3"><bdi>978-0-521-89293-3</bdi></a>. <q>That there exist in the world such entities as 'the religions' is an uncontroversial claim...However, it was not always so. The concepts 'religion' and 'the religions', as we presently understand them, emerged quite late in Western thought, during the Enlightenment. Between them, these two notions provided a new framework for classifying particular aspects of human life.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=%27Religion%27+and+the+Religions+in+the+English+Enlightenment&amp;rft.place=Cambridge&amp;rft.pages=1&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1990&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-521-89293-3&amp;rft.aulast=Harrison&amp;rft.aufirst=Peter&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Freligionreligion00harr&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFHarrison2015">Harrison, Peter (2015). <i>The Territories of Science and Religion</i>. University of Chicago Press. p. 101. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-226-18448-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-226-18448-7"><bdi>978-0-226-18448-7</bdi></a>. <q>The first recorded use of "Boudhism" was 1801, followed by "Hindooism" (1829), "Taouism" (1838), and "Confucianism" (1862) (see figure 6). By the middle of the nineteenth century these terms had secured their place in the English lexicon, and the putative objects to which they referred became permanent features of our understanding of the world.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Territories+of+Science+and+Religion&amp;rft.pages=101&amp;rft.pub=University+of+Chicago+Press&amp;rft.date=2015&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-226-18448-7&amp;rft.aulast=Harrison&amp;rft.aufirst=Peter&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Josephson_2-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Josephson_2_46-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Josephson_2_46-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFJosephson2012">Josephson, Jason Ananda (2012). <i>The Invention of Religion in Japan</i>. University of Chicago Press. p. 12. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-226-41234-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-226-41234-4"><bdi>978-0-226-41234-4</bdi></a>. <q>The early nineteenth century saw the emergence of much of this terminology, including the formation of the terms Boudhism (1801), Hindooism (1829), Taouism (1839), Zoroastri-anism (1854), and Confucianism (1862). This construction of "religions" was not merely the production of European translation terms, but the reification of systems of thought in a way strikingly divorced from their original cultural milieu. The original discovery of religions in different cultures was rooted in the assumption that each people had its own divine "revelation," or at least its own parallel to Christianity. In the same period, however, European and American explorers often suggested that specific African or Native American tribes lacked religion altogether. Instead these groups were reputed to have only superstitions and as such they were seen as less than human.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Invention+of+Religion+in+Japan&amp;rft.pages=12&amp;rft.pub=University+of+Chicago+Press&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-226-41234-4&amp;rft.aulast=Josephson&amp;rft.aufirst=Jason+Ananda&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFMorreallSonn2013">Morreall, John; Sonn, Tamara (2013). <i>50 Great Myths about Religions</i>. Wiley-Blackwell. p. 12. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-470-67350-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-470-67350-8"><bdi>978-0-470-67350-8</bdi></a>. <q>The phrase "World Religions" came into use when the first Parliament of the World's Religions was held in Chicago in 1893. Representation at the Parliament was not comprehensive. Naturally, Christians dominated the meeting, and Jews were represented. Muslims were represented by a single American Muslim. The enormously diverse traditions of India were represented by a single teacher, while three teachers represented the arguably more homogenous strains of Buddhist thought. The indigenous religions of the Americas and Africa were not represented. Nevertheless, since the convening of the Parliament, Judaism, Christianity, Islam, Hinduism, Buddhism, Confucianism, and Taoism have been commonly identified as World Religions. They are sometimes called the "Big Seven" in Religious Studies textbooks, and many generalizations about religion have been derived from them.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=50+Great+Myths+about+Religions&amp;rft.pages=12&amp;rft.pub=Wiley-Blackwell&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-470-67350-8&amp;rft.aulast=Morreall&amp;rft.aufirst=John&amp;rft.au=Sonn%2C+Tamara&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFRhodes1991">Rhodes, John (January 1991). "An American Tradition: The Religious Persecution of Native Americans". <i>Montana Law Review</i>. <b>52</b> (1): 13–72. <q>In their traditional languages, Native Americans have no word for religion. This absence is very revealing.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Montana+Law+Review&amp;rft.atitle=An+American+Tradition%3A+The+Religious+Persecution+of+Native+Americans&amp;rft.volume=52&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=13-72&amp;rft.date=1991-01&amp;rft.aulast=Rhodes&amp;rft.aufirst=John&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFMorreallSonn2013">Morreall, John; Sonn, Tamara (2013). <i>50 Great Myths about Religions</i>. Wiley-Blackwell. p. 14. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-470-67350-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-470-67350-8"><bdi>978-0-470-67350-8</bdi></a>. <q>Before the British colonized India, for example, the people there had no concept "religion" and no concept "Hinduism." There was no word "Hindu" in classical India, and no one spoke of "Hinduism" until the 1800s. Until the introduction of that term, Indians identified themselves by any number of criteria—family, trade or profession, or social level, and perhaps the scriptures they followed or the particular deity or deities upon whose care they relied in various contexts or to whom they were devoted. But these diverse identities were united, each an integral part of life; no part existed in a separate sphere identified as "religious." Nor were the diverse traditions lumped together under the term "Hinduism" unified by sharing such common features of religion as a single founder, creed, theology, or institutional organization.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=50+Great+Myths+about+Religions&amp;rft.pages=14&amp;rft.pub=Wiley-Blackwell&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-470-67350-8&amp;rft.aulast=Morreall&amp;rft.aufirst=John&amp;rft.au=Sonn%2C+Tamara&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-brian111-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-brian111_50-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation cs2" id="CITEREFPennington2005">Pennington, Brian K. (2005), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=7drluePK-acC&amp;pg=PA111" rel="nofollow"><i>Was Hinduism Invented?: Britons, Indians, and the Colonial Construction of Religion</i></a>, Oxford University Press, pp. 111–118, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-803729-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-803729-3"><bdi>978-0-19-803729-3</bdi></a>, <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20191217044908/https://books.google.com/books?id=7drluePK-acC&amp;pg=PA111" rel="nofollow">archived</a> from the original on 17 December 2019<span class="reference-accessdate">, retrieved <span class="nowrap">5 August</span> 2018</span></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Was+Hinduism+Invented%3F%3A+Britons%2C+Indians%2C+and+the+Colonial+Construction+of+Religion&amp;rft.pages=111-118&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-803729-3&amp;rft.aulast=Pennington&amp;rft.aufirst=Brian+K.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D7drluePK-acC%26pg%3DPA111&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFLloyd_Ridgeon2003">Lloyd Ridgeon (2003). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=HFKBAgAAQBAJ" rel="nofollow"><i>Major World Religions: From Their Origins to the Present</i></a>. Routledge. pp. 10–11. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-134-42935-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-134-42935-6"><bdi>978-1-134-42935-6</bdi></a>. <q>It is often said that Hinduism is very ancient, and in a sense this is true [...]. It was formed by adding the English suffix -ism, of Greek origin, to the word <i>Hindu</i>, of Persian origin; it was about the same time that the word <i>Hindu</i>, without the suffix -ism, came to be used mainly as a religious term. [...] The name <i>Hindu</i> was first a geographical name, not a religious one, and it originated in the languages of Iran, not of India. [...] They referred to the non-Muslim majority, together with their culture, as 'Hindu'. [...] Since the people called Hindu differed from Muslims most notably in religion, the word came to have religious implications, and to denote a group of people who were identifiable by their Hindu religion. [...] However, it is a religious term that the word <i>Hindu</i> is now used in English, and Hinduism is the name of a religion, although, as we have seen, we should beware of any false impression of uniformity that this might give us.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Major+World+Religions%3A+From+Their+Origins+to+the+Present&amp;rft.pages=10-11&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-134-42935-6&amp;rft.au=Lloyd+Ridgeon&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DHFKBAgAAQBAJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Invention_Japan-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Invention_Japan_52-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFJosephson2012">Josephson, Jason Ananda (2012). <i>The Invention of Religion in Japan</i>. University of Chicago Press. pp. 1, 11–12. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-226-41234-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-226-41234-4"><bdi>978-0-226-41234-4</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Invention+of+Religion+in+Japan&amp;rft.pages=1%2C+11-12&amp;rft.pub=University+of+Chicago+Press&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-226-41234-4&amp;rft.aulast=Josephson&amp;rft.aufirst=Jason+Ananda&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-japan_Galen-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-japan_Galen_53-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFZuckermanGalenPasquale2016">Zuckerman, Phil; Galen, Luke; Pasquale, Frank (2016). "2. Secularity around the World". <i>The Nonreligious: Understanding Secular People and Societies</i>. Oxford University Press. pp. 39–40. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-992494-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-992494-3"><bdi>978-0-19-992494-3</bdi></a>. <q>It was only in response to Western cultural contact in the late nineteenth century that a Japanese word for religion (shukyo) came into use. It tends to be associated with foreign, founded, or formally organized traditions, particularly Christianity and other monotheisms, but also Buddhism and new religious sects.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=2.+Secularity+around+the+World&amp;rft.btitle=The+Nonreligious%3A+Understanding+Secular+People+and+Societies&amp;rft.pages=39-40&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2016&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-992494-3&amp;rft.aulast=Zuckerman&amp;rft.aufirst=Phil&amp;rft.au=Galen%2C+Luke&amp;rft.au=Pasquale%2C+Frank&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Max_M%C3%BCller" title="Max Müller">Max Müller</a>, <i>Natural Religion</i>, p. 33, 1889</span>

<li id="cite_note-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-55">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.04.0059%3Aentry%3D%2340976" rel="nofollow">"Lewis &amp; Short, <i>A Latin Dictionary</i>"</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210226000346/http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.04.0059%3Aentry%3D%2340976" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 26 February 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. "Temporal lobe epilepsy in the priestly source of the Pentateuch". <i>South African Medical Journal</i>. <b>11</b> (94): 870. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/15587438" rel="nofollow">15587438</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=South+African+Medical+Journal&amp;rft.atitle=Temporal+lobe+epilepsy+in+the+priestly+source+of+the+Pentateuch&amp;rft.volume=11&amp;rft.issue=94&amp;rft.pages=870&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F15587438&amp;rft.aulast=Altschuler&amp;rft.aufirst=EL&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Heilman-102"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Heilman_102-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFHeilmanValenstein2011">Heilman, Kenneth M.; Valenstein, Edward (2011). <i>Clinical Neuropsychology</i>. Oxford University Press. p. 488. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-538487-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-538487-1"><bdi>978-0-19-538487-1</bdi></a>. <q>Studies that claim to show no difference in emotional makeup between temporal lobe and other epileptic patients (Guerrant et al., 1962; Stevens, 1966) have been reinterpreted (Blumer, 1975) to indicate that there is, in fact, a difference: those with temporal lobe epilepsy are more likely to have more serious forms of emotional disturbance. This typical personality of temporal lobe epileptic patient has been described in roughly similar terms over many years (Blumer &amp; Benson, 1975; Geschwind, 1975, 1977; Blumer, 1999; Devinsky &amp; Schachter, 2009). These patients are said to have a deepening of emotions; they ascribe great significance to commonplace events. This can be manifested as a tendency to take a cosmic view; hyperreligiosity (or intensely professed atheism) is said to be common.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Clinical+Neuropsychology&amp;rft.pages=488&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-538487-1&amp;rft.aulast=Heilman&amp;rft.aufirst=Kenneth+M.&amp;rft.au=Valenstein%2C+Edward&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-Spread-127"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Spread_127-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=g2AtOlJMPTUC&amp;pg=PA52" rel="nofollow"><i>Muslim-Christian Relations</i></a>. Simatupang said, if the number of the Christians were double or triple, then the number of the ministers should also be doubled or tripled and the role of the laity should be maximized and Christian service to society through schools, universities, hospitals and orphanages, should be increased. In addition, for him the Christian mission should be involved in the struggle for justice amid the process of modernization.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Muslim-Christian+Relations&amp;rft.pub=Amsterdam+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.isbn=978-90-5356-938-2&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Dg2AtOlJMPTUC%26pg%3DPA52&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Charity-128"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Charity_128-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFFred_Kammer2004">Fred Kammer (1 May 2004). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=WmuV6g0yR3sC&amp;pg=PA77" rel="nofollow"><i>Doing Faith Justice</i></a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paulist_Press" title="Paulist Press">Paulist Press</a>. p. 77. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8091-4227-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8091-4227-9"><bdi>978-0-8091-4227-9</bdi></a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210126184216/https://books.google.com/books?id=WmuV6g0yR3sC&amp;pg=PA77" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 26 January 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">18 October</span> 2007</span>. <q>Theologians, bishops, and preachers urged the Christian community to be as compassionate as their God was, reiterating that creation was for all of humanity. They also accepted and developed the identification of Christ with the poor and the requisite Christian duty to the poor. Religious congregations and individual charismatic leaders promoted the development of a number of helping institutions-hospitals, hospices for <a href="/wiki/Christian_pilgrimage" title="Christian pilgrimage">pilgrims</a>, orphanages, shelters for unwed mothers-that laid the foundation for the modern "large network of hospitals, orphanages and schools, to serve the poor and society at large."</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Doing+Faith+Justice&amp;rft.pages=77&amp;rft.pub=Paulist+Press&amp;rft.date=2004-05-01&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8091-4227-9&amp;rft.au=Fred+Kammer&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DWmuV6g0yR3sC%26pg%3DPA77&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Religion_and_Medicine-201"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Religion_and_Medicine_201-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFMuellerPlevakRummans2001">Mueller, Paul S.; Plevak, David J.; Rummans, Teresa A. (2001). <a class="external text" href="https://www.mayoclinicproceedings.org/article/S0025-6196(11)62799-7/pdf" rel="nofollow">"Religious Involvement, Spirituality, and Medicine: Implications for Clinical Practice"</a>. <i>Mayo Clinic Proceedings</i>. <b>76</b> (12): 1225–1235. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.4065%2F76.12.1225" rel="nofollow">10.4065/76.12.1225</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/11761504" rel="nofollow">11761504</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20191108220203/https://www.mayoclinicproceedings.org/article/S0025-6196(11)62799-7/pdf" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 8 November 2019<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">13 November</span> 2010</span>. <q>We reviewed published studies, meta-analyses, systematic reviews, and subject reviews that examined the association between religious involvement and spirituality and physical health, mental health, health-related quality of life, and other health outcomes. We also reviewed articles that provided suggestions on how clinicians might assess and support the spiritual needs of patients. Most studies have shown that religious involvement and spirituality are associated with better health outcomes, including greater longevity, coping skills, and health-related quality of life (even during terminal illness) and less anxiety, depression, and suicide</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Mayo+Clinic+Proceedings&amp;rft.atitle=Religious+Involvement%2C+Spirituality%2C+and+Medicine%3A+Implications+for+Clinical+Practice&amp;rft.volume=76&amp;rft.issue=12&amp;rft.pages=1225-1235&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.4065%2F76.12.1225&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F11761504&amp;rft.aulast=Mueller&amp;rft.aufirst=Paul+S.&amp;rft.au=Plevak%2C+David+J.&amp;rft.au=Rummans%2C+Teresa+A.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.mayoclinicproceedings.org%2Farticle%2FS0025-6196%2811%2962799-7%2Fpdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>
</li>

(February 2001). "The Role of Religion and Spirituality in Mental and Physical Health". <i>Current Directions in Psychological Science</i>. <b>10</b> (1): 21–24. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2F1467-8721.00106" rel="nofollow">10.1111/1467-8721.00106</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:144109851" rel="nofollow">144109851</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Current+Directions+in+Psychological+Science&amp;rft.atitle=The+Role+of+Religion+and+Spirituality+in+Mental+and+Physical+Health&amp;rft.volume=10&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=21-24&amp;rft.date=2001-02&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1111%2F1467-8721.00106&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A144109851%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft.aulast=Seybold&amp;rft.aufirst=Kevin+S.&amp;rft.au=Hill%2C+Peter+C.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-CruelCreeds1-209"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-CruelCreeds1_209-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFEller2010">Eller, Jack David (2010). <i>Cruel Creeds, Virtuous Violence: Religious Violence Across Culture and History</i>. Prometheus Books. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-61614-218-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-61614-218-6"><bdi>978-1-61614-218-6</bdi></a>. <q>As we have insisted previously, religion is not inherently and irredeemably violent; it certainly is not the essence and source of all violence.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Cruel+Creeds%2C+Virtuous+Violence%3A+Religious+Violence+Across+Culture+and+History&amp;rft.pub=Prometheus+Books&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-61614-218-6&amp;rft.aulast=Eller&amp;rft.aufirst=Jack+David&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-CruelCreeds2-210"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-CruelCreeds2_210-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFEller2010">Eller, Jack David (2010). <i>Cruel Creeds, Virtuous Violence: Religious Violence Across Culture and History</i>. Prometheus Books. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-61614-218-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-61614-218-6"><bdi>978-1-61614-218-6</bdi></a>. <q>Religion and violence are clearly compatible, but they are not identical. Violence is one phenomenon in human (and natural existence), religion is another, and it is inevitable that the two would become intertwined. Religion is complex and modular, and violence is one of the modules—not universal, but recurring. As a conceptual and behavioral module, violence is by no means exclusive to religion. There are plenty of other groups, institutions, interests, and ideologies to promote violence. Violence is, therefore, neither essential to nor exclusive to religion. Nor is religious violence all alike... And virtually every form of religious violence has its nonreligious corollary.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Cruel+Creeds%2C+Virtuous+Violence%3A+Religious+Violence+Across+Culture+and+History&amp;rft.pub=Prometheus+Books&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-61614-218-6&amp;rft.aulast=Eller&amp;rft.aufirst=Jack+David&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReligion"></span></span>
</li>

(1980). <i>N.B</i>.: The first ed. appeared in 1949, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780023884306" title="Special:BookSources/9780023884306">9780023884306</a>. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.worldcat.org/oclc/4665144" rel="nofollow">4665144</a>.</li>
